==PAGE:1==
Dynamic modeling of separation of
fermentation liquids
By:
Jess Bjørn Rasmussen
s134489
Friday 1st June, 2018
==PAGE:2==
June, 2018
Abstract
In this thesis a rate based dynamic model for the separation of a fermentation liquid
in a horizontal heat integrated distillation tank is developed. The developed model is
implemented and simulated in Matlab Simulink.
The development of the model (both steady state and dynamic) is done in phases: First
a single compartment model is made. Then an adiabatic complete tank model and ﬁnally
a diabatic tank model. It was found that a total of 550 kW could be saved on the heat
duties for the condenser and reboiler when going from adiabatic to diabatic operation,
without altering the product compositions and ﬂow rates signiﬁcantly.
With a feed ﬂow rate of 5 mol/s ethanol and 95 mol/s water in the heat integrated
horizontal tank, it is possible to separate the fermentation liquid into a heavy and a
light product. The heavy product contains 1 mol% ethanol and with a ﬂow rate of 95.1
mol/s and the light product contains 81.2 mol% and a ﬂow rate of 4.9 mol/s. In order to
achieve these product streams high reﬂux and boilup ratios were required, 55.4 and 1.2
respectively.
In order to simplify the problem some simpliﬁcations and assumptions are made. E.g.
the fermentation liquid is assumed to be a binary mixture of water and ethanol. Another
assumption is the mass and heat transfer rate coeﬃcients could be determined by correla-
tions for randomly packed columns. This assumption implies there is mixing of the liquid
and vapor phases.
With the developed dynamic model of a heat integrated horizontal distillation tank distur-
bances to the feed ethanol composition (+20% and +5%) and ﬂow rate (+5% and +1%)
are investigated to evaluate whether the system is stable for large and small disturbances
in the feed. It was found that even small disturbances causes the system to be unstable,
which proves that a control conﬁguration is necessary. A strategy for an implementation
of a control conﬁguration is given based on the top-down bottom-up procedure. The
instability of the dynamic model with regards to disturbances is found to be linked with
the condenser holdup, so that when the condenser holdup decreases the system becomes
unstable. It is therefore important to control the condenser holdup in order to avoid
drifting outputs.
i
==PAGE:3==
Resumé
June, 2018
Resumé
I denne afhandling er der udviklet en dynamisk ikke-ligevægts baseret model for sepa-
rationen af fermenteringsvæsker i en horizontal varmeintegreret distillationstank. Den
udviklede model er blevet implementeret og simuleret i Matlab Simulink.
Udviklingen af den dynamiske model er gjort i ﬂere trin. Først er et enkelt afsnit af tanken
blevet modelleret, så en adiabatisk model og til sidst en komplet diabatisk model med
kondenser, kedel, kompressor og trykventil. Både dynamiske og statiske simuleringer er
kørt. Ved at gå fra en adiabatisk model til en diabatisk model blev det fundet at 550 kW
kunne spares på energikravene til kondenseren og kedelen.
Med en fødestrøm på 100 mol/s indeholdende 5 mol% ethanol og 95 mol% vand, kunne
en separation af ethanol-vand blandingen foregå i den varmeintegrerede horisontale tank,
hvorved man opnår et tungt produkt med 1 mol% ethanol og et let produkt med 81.2
mol% ethanol. Dog krævede dette store tilbagestrømninger fra kondenseren og kedelen.
Visse antagelser blev taget for at simpliﬁcere problemet, deriblandt er det antaget at
væske- og dampfaserne er tilstrækkeligt blandet for at sikre et højt overﬂadeareal, hvor
masseoverførelse kan ske.
Dynamiske simuleringer er blevet undersøgt for to forskellige ændringer i ethanol in-
dholdet i fødestrømmen (+20% og +5%) og to forskellige ændringer i fødestrømmens
molære hastighed (+5% og +1%).
Ændringerne er i forhold til fødestrømsværdierne
nævnt foroven.
Ud fra disse simuleringer er det påvist at for selv små ændringer fra
steady state værdierne i fødestrømmen bliver systemet ustabilt. Især det lette produkt
blev påvirket af disse ændringer. For at undgå ustabil operation er det derfor anbefalet
at implementere en reguleringsstrategi, og idéer til hvordan dette kan gøres er også givet
i denne afhandling. Det er især når molmængden i kondenseren falder at systemet bliver
ustabilt, og det er da også derfor vigtigt at styre denne variabel for at undgå en ustabil
proces.

1. Introduction
June, 2018
1
Introduction
With dwindling fossil fuel reserves new types of renewable fuel is necessary.
Such a
renewable fuel is ethanol, which can be made by fermentation of starchy, cellulose or
saccharine biological materials [1]. At this moment, ethanol is not used as a fuel on its
own, but as a way to decrease the gasoline consumption in vehicles. In USA there is
on average 10 vol% ethanol in gasoline [2], this ethanol content is compatible with all
newer gasoline engine vehicles. In Brazil it is mandatory for the commercially available
gasoline to contain at least 27.5 vol% ethanol [3]. In Denmark commercial gasoline is by
law required to contain at least 5.75 vol% ethanol, based on criteria from the European
Union [4]. Some special vehicles, the so-called ﬂex-fuel vehicles, can run on gasoline with
a ethanol content of up to 85 vol% [2].
Studies have shown that the combustion of ethanol compared to gasoline have lower NOx,
CO2 and particle emissions based on grams of equivalent CO2 per mega Joule fuel [5][6].
The use of ethanol as a gasoline substitute, or at least as an additive, therefore reduces
some of the harmful emissions compared to pure gasoline fuels, as well as being a renewable
fuel.
Bioethanol is produced by ﬁrst pretreating the biomaterial, then ferment it and ﬁnally
distillate the ethanol from the fermentation liquid [1]. It is the sugar molecules in the
biomaterial which is fermented to produce ethanol.
The pretreatment is needed to make the sugar molecules more accessible for the fermen-
tation process to take place. Diﬀerent techniques for the pretreatment is possible, e.g.
steam treatment or extraction [7]. During the pretreatment the biomaterial is separated
into a solid and a liquid phase. The solid phase contains the long ﬁbers, such as cellulose
and starch, while the liquid phase contains the small sugary molecules. The solid ﬁbers
can be liqueﬁed and, along with the liquid sugars, fermented [7].
During the fermentation yeast is added to promote the production of ethanol. Gaseous
CO2 is also formed, which is inert so it does not aﬀect the ethanol production. After
fermentation is complete the fermentation liquid mainly contains water, ethanol and solid
residue from cells and yeast. The ethanol concentration after the fermentation depends
on which type of yeast and biomaterial is used and can range between 8.2 g/L and 96.6
g/L [8]. The residue can be removed and used as livestock fodder [1]. It is possible that
the fermentation process also yields some minor byproducts, such as other alcohols or
esters, these are typically in lower concentrations than ethanol [1].
There are some issues regarding the bioethanol production. To produce bioethanol there
is a need of a lot of biomaterial. This could cause issues with regards to transporting
raw material to and products from a plant [9]. With smaller and mobile plants instead
of a large plant, it would be possible to solve some of these transportation issues. For
Page 1 of 100
==PAGE:10==
1. Introduction
June, 2018
example instead of producing bioethanol at one location and ship it over large distances
the bioethanol could be made and used locally. Another issue when producing bioethanol
is the thermal degradation of enzymes or yeast used for the fermentation. Therefore, the
temperature of the ethanol distillation should be kept low [9].
The fermentation liquid contains a high volume fraction of water, dependent on the type
of biomaterial [1].
Adiabatic distillation processes generally consumes high quantities
of energy to operate the reboiler and condenser. With high water concentrations the
separation of ethanol can therefore be very expensive. This expensive separation therefore
makes the ethanol expensive. If ethanol is to be a reasonable alternative to gasoline, the
market value must match, or be lower than that of gasoline. If the distillation process
could be made cheaper, by lowering the energy consumption, the ethanol price could
be set lower and thus ethanol could be a cheaper, renewable and less harmful to the
environment alternative to gasoline.
Traditionally, vertical distillation columns with trays, a reboiler at the bottom and a
condenser at the top, have been the standard in distillation processing. These columns
are well tested and used as an industry standard. They are usually run adiabatically across
the column, which means the reboiler and condenser can have high energy consumption.
Fermentation liquids contain solid residue which could pose diﬃculties in traditional
columns with the blocking of trays.
Alternative methods for cheaper distillations are
being investigated. One suggestion is the zero-gravity distillation, or micro-distillation, in
which the liquid phase is inside a porous medium and the liquid ﬂow comes from capillary
forces [10][11]. An advantage of this zero-gravity distillation is smaller distillation setup,
and the heat duty can therefore be lowered. However, this technique is not suitable for
fermentation liquids, since the solid residue in the liquid can easily block the pores which
have dimensions in orders of millimeters [11].
Currently, it is investigated whether the energy consumption can be lowered by heat
integrating the distillation column [12][13]. These newer distillation techniques does not
have any internal trays, which is an advantage for fermentation liquids, since it prevents
the solid material buildup.
One new way of lowering the heat consumption is the vertical heat integrated distillation
column (HIDiC) [12]. This vertical distillation column has an inner stripping section and
an outer rectifying section and no trays. The rectifying section is hotter than the stripping
section, because the feed into the rectifying section is pressurized, and acts as a diabatic
heat integration, which lowers the overall energy consumption. The more complex double
layer column will have a lower utility cost, since the column is heat integrated, however
it has a higher capital cost, due to the complexity of the design.
Another novel type of distillation is the horizontal diabatic distillation tank [13]. This
tank is similar to the heat integrated distillation column, but instead of being vertical it
Page 2 of 100
==PAGE:11==
1. Introduction
June, 2018
is horizontal. The principle is the same with the inner stripping section and the outer
rectifying section, and as in the vertical HIDiC the energy consumption can be lowered
compared to the traditional columns. One of the main diﬀerences in the two designs is
the ﬂow of the vapor and liquid phase. In the vertical tank the inner walls are wet by
the liquid phase while the vapor phase is in the middle. In the horizontal tank only the
bottom of the inner walls are wet by the liquid phase due to gravity with the vapor phase
above it. If it is necessary or preferred, a mechanical propeller or similar can be inserted
in the horizontal tank, and thus the mixing of the two phases can be improved.
The two heat integrated distillation techniques described above can possibly be used in
the bioethanol production, to lower the energy consumption and avoid problems with solid
residues. However, the heat integrated tanks are not as simple to build as the traditional
columns, so there would be an increase in the capital cost. If the overall production cost,
with an increase in capital cost and a decrease in energy cost, for the new heat integrated
distillation techniques is lower than compared to a traditional column, the bioethanol
product price can be lowered. This way it can be a more economic reasonable alternative
to gasoline.
One reason why the horizontal tank could be an advantage over the HIDiC is it is easier
to transport a horizontal set up compared to a vertical. So if it is wanted to move the
process, either from plant to plant or inside a plant, a horizontal tank would be preferred.
Schematics of the horizontal distillation tank are shown in Figure 1.1.
Figure 1.1: Schematics of horizontal tank with heat integration.
The ﬁgure shows the feed input at one end of the tank. However, in theory it could be
placed anywhere. The vapor leaving the tank is compressed before being sent back in an
outer shell. The increase in pressure leads to an increase in temperature, which in turn
heats the inner tank. The vapor leaving the outer shell is condensed and returned to the
Page 3 of 100
==PAGE:12==
2. Theoretical background
June, 2018
outer shell. The liquid is sent back into the tank with the feed. It is assumed a liquid
ﬁlm will form on the inside and outside of the inner tank wall.
This horizontal distillation can be an alternative to previous separation techniques of
ethanol from fermentation liquids. A mathematical model of the distillation process can be
used to investigate whether a horizontal distillation set up is a valid option for separation
of ethanol from fermentation liquid. With an accurate model describing the process it
is possible to investigate scenarios without performing time consuming and expensive
experiments. However, in order to validate the model experimental results are required
for comparison with the model results.
With a dynamic model it is further possible to investigate the eﬀect of disturbances on
the system and implement a control conﬁguration design.
2
Theoretical background
The typical rigorous modeling of a distillation process involves dividing the column into
stages, where the condenser is stage 1 and the reboiler stage N. Over the years more
advanced models have been developed to describe the distillation.
Equilibrium based
models can be used for simple distillation processes, while more complex processes can be
modeled by a nonequilibrium based model.
All these types of models depend on certain properties, which depend on the model
variables. A full model to describe the distillation must include these properties and their
dependencies, to get the most accurate description.
The equilibrium and nonequilibrium models in steady state consist of a nonlinear system
with n nonlinear algebraic expressions and n unknown variables. To solve such a system
an iterative method, like the Newton method, can be used based on an initial guess. The
dynamic models contain ordinary diﬀerential equations as well as nonlinear expressions.
A system of diﬀerential and algebraic equations can not be solved only by using Newton’s
method and an alternative method must be used.
This section shows the available models for distillation processes and a description of the
horizontal tank distillation. The way to solve these models is also represented as well as
the theory behind the design of a control strategy for a distillation process.
2.1
Equilibrium based modeling
One of the ﬁrst ways to make a rigorous model for a multistage distillation process was
the so-called equilibrium modeling. This model is developed for tray columns and it is
Page 4 of 100
==PAGE:13==
2.1. Equilibrium based modeling
June, 2018
assumed each tray, or stage, is in equilibrium [14]. It is rarely true that every stage is an
equilibrium stage, which means the results from these models needs to be adjusted. One
way to adjust the results is to use an eﬃciency factor, such as the Murphree eﬃciency or
a stage eﬃciency like the HETP (height equivalent to a theoretical plate) [14].
The equilibrium based models are comprised of the MESH equations (material balances,
equilibrium relations, summation equations and heat balance) for every stage [14]. Figure
2.1 shows the variables for a general equilibrium stage.
Figure 2.1: Equilibrium stage j.
The MESH equations for stage j are as follows, from Seader et al. [15].
Material balance for component i. With C components this gives C equations.
Mi,j ≡Lj−1xi,j−1 + Vj+1yi,j+1 + Fjzi,j −(Lj + Uj)xi,j −(Vj + Wj)yi,j = 0
(2.1.1)
This is a general steady state mass balance with molar ﬂow from previous stages in vapor
Vj+1 and liquid Lj−1 phases, with a composition of yi,j+1 and xi,j−1 of component i in
each respective phase. Furthermore, a feed ﬂow Fj with a composition zi,j also enters the
stage. The inlet component ﬂow rates must be equal to the outlet ﬂow rates for a steady
state case. The liquid ﬂow rate outlet Lj and vapor outlet Vj continues to the next or
Page 5 of 100
==PAGE:14==
2.2. Horizontal distillation
June, 2018
previous stage. Flow rates Uj and Wj are removed as side streams from the stage.
The equilibrium equation for component i:
Ei,j ≡yi,j −Ki,jxi,j = 0
(2.1.2)
The two phase compositions must be in equilibrium, this is a criteria for using the equi-
librium model. The K-value is the equilibrium ratio and can be found by using Raoult’s
law for ideal systems and a modiﬁed Raoult’s law for non-ideal systems.
The phase equilibrium ratio Ki,j is dependent on temperature, pressure and composition.
The summation equations are:
(Sy)j ≡
C
X
i=1
yi,j −1 = 0
(2.1.3)
(Sx)j ≡
C
X
i=1
xi,j −1 = 0
(2.1.4)
The heat balance for stage j is:
Hj ≡Lj−1HL
j−1 + Vj+1HV
j+1 + FjHF
j
−(Lj + Uj)HL
j −(Vj + Wj)HV
j −Qj = 0
(2.1.5)
Like the material balance the heat balance is in steady state, without an accumulation
term, meaning the inlet energy must be equal to the output energy.
The enthalpies depend on temperature, pressure and compositions, and as for the K-
values, they are property relations. When these properties are found, there are NE =
2C + 3 equations and NV = N(3C + 10) + 1 variables, where N is the number of
stages. This gives a degree of freedom of DOF = NV −NE = N(C + 7) + 1. Speci-
fying N, Fj, zi,j, TF,j, PF,j, Pj, Uj, Wj and Qj means the remaining variables xi,j, yi,j, Lj, Vj
and Tj can be found.
The MESH equations are nonlinear, which means an iterative
method is needed to solve the system.
2.2
Horizontal distillation
As mentioned previously a new distillation technique involving a horizontal distillation
tank is being developed [13].
The equilibrium based model above is mainly suitable
for traditional distillation processes, and it is rather impractical due to the need of a
tray eﬃciency [15]. For complex distillation processes it is often not accurate enough to
simulate real world applications [14][16]. Complex distillation processes include reactive
and extractive distillation as well as novel designs of distillation columns.
Page 6 of 100
==PAGE:15==
2.2. Horizontal distillation
June, 2018
The fermentation liquid is expensive to separate in a traditional column. To make op-
eration cheaper heat integrated columns can be used. A vertical heat integrated column
could be used as a distillation column. This case have been modeled and investigated pre-
viously [12]. The heat integrated column could also be oriented horizontal. By orienting
the tank horizontally instead, the ﬂows of the phases change, which in turn changes the
mass and heat transfer dynamics of the separation.
Traditional distillation columns have trays inside, which deﬁnes the stage. In the horizon-
tal tank there are no trays, so instead the tank is divided into theoretical compartments
representing a stage in the model. The condenser is deﬁned as stage, or compartment, 1
and the reboiler is deﬁned as stage N. A compartment representation of the distillation
vessel is shown in Figure 2.2.
Figure 2.2: Compartment representation of the distillation tank shown in Figure 1.1.
The hot vapor and liquid in the outer shell is transferring its heat to the inner tank. The
liquid can be sent back into the tank with the feed, while the vapor is condensed and
sent to the outer shell. The heavy product is removed as a fraction of the ﬂow from the
reboiler. The light product is removed as a fraction of the ﬂow from the condenser.
To simplify the problem the separation in the inner tank and outer shell can ﬁrst be
assumed to be adiabatic, and then later the heat transfer between the two parts can be
added. Figure 2.3 shows a single double layer (inner tank and outer shell) stage in the
tank.
Page 7 of 100
==PAGE:16==
2.2. Horizontal distillation
June, 2018
Figure 2.3: Single double layer stage in the horizontal heat integrated tank.
The liquid will form a thin ﬁlm on the inner tube walls, a cross-sectional view of the tank
is shown in Figure 2.4, where the ﬁlm can be seen. The dimensions in the ﬁgure are not
to scale.
Figure 2.4: Cross-sectional view of the horizontal distillation tank.
The energy transfer from the outer shell to the inner tank is through a steel wall. There-
Page 8 of 100
==PAGE:17==
2.3. Rate based modeling
June, 2018
fore, the heat transfer must be determined from the conduction through the wall. For
simplicity the wall could be assumed to be very thin with no heat transfer resistance.
Instead of compartmentalizing the tank the whole tank could be modeled as one. By
doing so the mass and material balances must describe the entire tank, as has been done
previously for a vertical HIDiC [12]. To get a solution the model was discretized across the
tank length. By dividing the tank into compartments, where certain variables (ﬂow rates,
phase compositions and temperatures) are constant, there is no need for discretization of
the variables. Furthermore, by dividing the tank into compartments, the system becomes
easier to solve. With the compartment modeling, the steady state case becomes a system
of nonlinear algebraic equations instead of a diﬀerential algebraic equation system, and
the dynamic case can be described by a diﬀerential algebraic equation system instead of
a partial diﬀerential equation system.
Since the equilibrium based model is insuﬃcient to describe complex distillation processes,
such as the one shown above, another model must be used. Such a model is the more
complex rate based model.
2.3
Rate based modeling
For the rate based models, also known as nonequilibrium models, it is assumed that there
is only equilibrium in a stage at the vapor-liquid interface. The models are based on mass
and heat transfer rates [15]. Taylor et al. developed a rate based model in 1985 [17]. One
advantage of the rate base models is that eﬃciencies are no longer needed to describe
the actual behavior in the distillation process as well as being able of describing complex
processes. The rate based models are usually used for tray columns and packed columns.
Because of the stage no longer being assumed to be in equilibrium, it is a very general
and versatile model.
The rate based models are composed of the MERSHQ equations (material balances,
energy balances, rate equations, summation equations, hydraulic equation and equilibrium
equations) for each stage [14]. Figure 2.5 shows a general nonequilibrium stage j for the
rate based model with the corresponding variables.
Page 9 of 100
==PAGE:18==
2.3. Rate based modeling
June, 2018
Figure 2.5: Nonequilibrium stage j.
It is common practice to have the material and energy transfer rates being positive when
it goes from vapor to liquid, and negative for liquid to vapor [15].
2.3.1

and Seader et al. [15] have previously shown the model equations for a steady state case,

2.3.2

The above steady state model is good to describe the system when there are no holdups,
however in real life this is not always the case. To account for the holdups a dynamic model
is needed. Edgar, Eldridge & Peng has derived a dynamic model for a packed reactive
distillation column [18] and Baur, Taylor & Krishna a model for a reactive distillation
[19]. For a dynamic model, it is no longer assumed there are no mass and energy holdup.

It is also possible to set up a similar dynamic model for the equilibrium model, with
material and energy holdups.
This dynamic model both contain diﬀerential expressions for the material and energy
holdups and algebraic expressions for the interface equations. Such a diﬀerential-algebraic-
equation (DAE) system can not be solved as the steady state models, and alternative
methods must be used [20].

2.4

As mentioned previously, there are some physical properties which depends on the vari-
ables. The physical properties are: The equilibrium ratio K, the mass transfer rates N P
i,j,

2.4.1
Equilibrium ratio
The separation factor can be found using a γ-φ model. Where γ is the activity coeﬃcient
and φ is the fugacity. Furthermore, the saturation pressure is also needed.
Ki,j = P sat

composition.
The saturation pressure depends on the temperature and can be found from Antoine’s
equation. Since the equilibrium only is at the interface, it is the interface compositions
and temperature which are needed for the calculation of K. The stage pressure is also
needed, but this is speciﬁed.

Enthalpies depend on temperature. Generally the enthalpy for component i at a temper-

If the heat capacity is assumed to be temperature independent then the enthalpy is found
from:
Hi(T) = Hi(Tref) + Cp(T)(T −Tref)
(2.4.19)
Reference values for enthalpies and heat capacities can be found in literature, e.g. on the
U.S. National Institute of Standards and Technology’s website [25] or the CRC Handbook
of Chemistry and Physics [26].
In the distillation process it is the vaporization enthalpy that is of interest.
2.4.5
Concentrations
The concentrations in each phase can be found from the volumetric ﬂow rate and the
molar ﬂow rate.
cL
T,j =
Lj
ΦL,jacuL
(2.4.20)
cV
T,j =
Vj
(1 −ΦL,j)acuV
(2.4.21)
Where ΦL,j is the volumetric liquid ﬁlling fraction of a stage and ac is the cross-sectional
area of the tank. If the tank is assumed to be half full of liquid then ΦL,j = 1/2. The
volumetric ﬂow rate is the linear velocity times the cross-sectional area multiplied by the
ﬁlling fraction of either liquid or vapor.
Alternatively, since the vapor is assumed ideal, the vapor concentration can be found from
the ideal gas law.
2.5
Solving the steady state and dynamic models
As previously mentioned the models above are nonlinear, and the solving of these can be
diﬃcult. For a steady state model Newton’s method can be used. For a dynamic model,
where diﬀerential equations are present, the Newton’s method can no longer be used and
an alternative such as the Runge-Kutta method must be used. The Runge-Kutta method
is a way of numerical integrating a system, so in order to integrate it, the initial conditions
and known input variables are needed.
2.5.1
Newton’s method
If F(X) = F denotes a vector containing the nonlinear system functions and X a vector
of the unknown variables, the solution to the system is when F = 0.
Page 17 of 100
==PAGE:26==
2.5. Solving the steady state and dynamic models
June, 2018
The Newton method is a way of guessing the variable values from an initial guess. If k is
the current guess, then the next guess can be found as [17]:
Xk+1 = Xk −[Jk]−1F
(2.5.1)
Where [Jk] is the Jacobian matrix, where each element is deﬁned as:
Jij = ∂Fi
∂Xj
(2.5.2)
The solution of X is found by deﬁning an initial guess X0, ﬁnd the next and the next and
so on, until the system is converged. The convergence criteria are [27]:
v
u
u
u
t
N
X

eq
X

F 2
ij < ε
or
N
X

eq
X

|Xij+1 −Xij|
Xij
< ε
(2.5.3)
Where eq is the number of equations for each stage j and ε is the convergence number,
a small number for which the system is said to be converged. A low ε will give more
accurate results, but also a long computing time.
The Jacobian matrix can at times be diﬃcult to ﬁnd, since there are many variable
dependencies in the functions. The derivative can be estimated using the central diﬀerence
method [17]:
∂Fi
∂xj
= Fi(xj + ε) −Fi(xj −ε)
ε
(2.5.4)
2.5.2
Runge-Kutta method
For a system of both diﬀerential and algebraic equations (DAE systems), the Newton
method can no longer be applied to ﬁnd the solutions, since it does not take the diﬀerential
terms into account. The Runge-Kutta method can be used to solve such a system [20].
To use this method the diﬀerential terms in this method must have constant coeﬃcient.
The semi-implicit Runge-Kutta method have been used for these kinds of systems, since
it is a stable method [19][20]. The semi-implicit third order Runge-Kutta method was
described by Michelsen [28].
The diﬀerential equation system is deﬁned as:
dX
dt = F
(2.5.5)
With initial elements X(tn) = Xn. The solution at tn + h, where h > 0 is the step size,
is deﬁned as:
Xk+1 = Xk + R1k1 + R2k2 + k3
(2.5.6)
Page 18 of 100
==PAGE:27==
2.6. Control strategy
June, 2018
Where the k vectors are found from:
k1 = h([I] −ha[Jn])−1F(Xn)
(2.5.7)
k1 = h([I] −ha[Jn])−1F(Xn + b2k1)
(2.5.8)
k1 = ([I] −ha[Jn])−1(b31k1 + b32k2)
(2.5.9)
The remaining parameters are deﬁned as: a3 −3a2 + 3/2 a −1/6 = 0 ⇒a = 0.4358,
b2 = 0.75, b31 = −1/(6a)·(8a2−2a+1) = −0.6302, b32 = 2/(9a)·(6a2−6a+1) = −0.2423,
R1 = 11/27 −b31 = 1.0376 and R2 = 16/27 −b32 = 0.8349.
The Jacobian matrix is only evaluated once at the initial conditions [J(tn)] = [Jn], and
the function is only evaluated at one intermediate step F(Xn + b2k1).
It is possible to include step length adjustments for each step [28].
The Runge-Kutta method must be solved simultaneously with Newton’s method to solve
the DAE system.
These methods, or similar, for solving nonlinear and DAE systems are integrated in
process simulation software, such as Aspen Plus, and in mathematical simulation tools
like Matlab Simulink. Sometimes other methods than the ones described above is used,
but the idea is the same, by starting from an initial guess and then converging to a
solution.
For implementation in Matlab the functions fsolve, lsqnonlin and fmincon all use a New-
tonian method to solve nonlinear systems. In Simulink an algebraic loop can be used to
these nonlinear algebraic equation system. For the dynamic model it is a good idea to
ﬁrst solve the steady state model with the holdups, and use the steady state values as
initial values for the diﬀerential equation system.
2.6
Control strategy
The design of a control strategy for any process is often a diﬃcult task. Chemical plants
contain many measurements and actuators, and the conﬁguration of the optimal control
is not easy to ﬁnd. Larsson and Skogestad have proposed a top-down bottom-up anal-
ysis to ﬁnd controlled and manipulated variables for a plantwide control structure [29].
Furthermore, Bisgaard et al. [30] have shown a control strategy for a vertical heat inte-
grated concentric distillation column, similar to the horizontal tank described earlier. This
control strategy is also based on the top-down bottom-up analysis. The top-down anal-
ysis contains steady state considerations while the bottom-up analysis contains dynamic
considerations.
The top-down analysis is the ﬁrst part of designing a control structure. First the controlled
Page 19 of 100
==PAGE:28==
2.6. Control strategy
June, 2018
variables are found.
To ﬁnd these, ﬁrst the operational objective and constraints are
deﬁned. Then the number of steady state degrees of freedom is found:
DOFSS = Nactuators −Noutputs,0 −Ninputs,0
(2.6.1)
Where Nactuators is the number of actuators, e.g. valves, Noutputs,0 is the number of outputs
with no steady state eﬀect and likewise Ninputs,0 is the number of inputs with no steady
state eﬀect.
The DOFSS gives how many simulation inputs that are needed for the steady state opti-
mization, and therefore also the number of controlled variables.
The primary controlled variables CV1 are then identiﬁed, by ﬁnding the solution to a
optimization problem. The optimization problem could be to minimize the overall cost
of operation, while being subject to product composition constraints and equipment con-
straints. With the solution of this optimization the optimal steady state operation is
found. The controlled variables, CV1, are often chosen as the active constraints. The
active constraint for a variable, is when the optimal variable is the same as the constraint
limit, e.g.
if the optimal temperature is the maximum temperature allowed then the
temperature is an active constraints.
The ﬁnal step of the top-down analysis is to determine the throughput manipulator, TPM.
This can either be the feed ﬂow rate or one of the product ﬂow rates. The location of the
TPM is where the production rate is adjusted. It is in general a good idea to place the
TPM at a bottleneck, if the throughput is to be maximized [30].
The bottom-up analysis is then used to design the regulatory and supervisory control
layers. The regulatory layer is implemented to avoid drifting in the system, by controlling
drifting control variables CV2.
The CV2 are selected and paired with a manipulated
variable MV2.
It is a good idea to pair a controlled variable close to a manipulated
variable.
The regulatory control layer can then be implemented with CV2 and MV2. Once the
regulatory layer has been made it can be ordered in terms of how diﬃcult the tuning is.
Some controllers have little interactions with others, such as level controllers, and are easy
to tune, while some controllers have high interactions and are diﬃcult to tune.
On top of the regulatory control layer, the supervisory control layer is implemented. This
is the primary controlled variables, CV1, which are paired and coordinated.
Rules and guidelines for choosing the correct controlled variables, optimal TPM location
and structure of control layers are given by Minasidis et al. [31].
Page 20 of 100
==PAGE:29==
3. Problem description
June, 2018
3
Problem description
The horizontal tank in this project, shown in Figure 1.1 and Figure 2.4, has a diameter
of 2 m and length of 6 m. It is assumed the outer shell has a thickness of 10 cm from
the inner tank wall to the outer shell wall. Assuming the wall thickness is negligible, this
means the inner tank has a diameter of 1.8 m. These dimensions are chosen so the tank
can ﬁt in a shipping container and thus the tank can easily be transported either within
a plant or from location to location [32].
A double layer stage j in the horizontal tank is shown in Figure 3.1, with the inlets and
outlets from the nonequilibrium model. This ﬁgure is a combination of the stage in Figure
2.3 and the nonequilibrium stage in Figure 2.5. It is assumed there is no heat transfer to
or from the surroundings and no side streams.
Page 21 of 100
==PAGE:30==
3. Problem description
June, 2018
Figure 3.1: A single double layer stage j in the horizontal tank. No heat transfer to or
from the surroundings and no side streams.
For simpliﬁcation the heat transfer across the inner tank wall can initially be neglected.
This simpliﬁcation makes the distillation process an adiabatic process. To further simplify
the heat integration it is assumed there is a liquid ﬁlm on both sides of the inner tank
wall, as shown on the ﬁgure above.
By combining multiple nonequilibrium double layer stages as the one shown in Figure 3.1
and a reboiler and a condenser, a full distillation process can be modeled and simulated.
For the modeling the stage is divided in two compartments: the inner tank and the outer
shell. This means for the complete tank model each double layer stage consists of two
Page 22 of 100
==PAGE:31==
3.1. Model assumptions
June, 2018
compartments and the heat transfer between the two.
The tank is divided in to a total of 30 stages, with the inner tank and outer shell, this
gives 60 compartments, each being modeled by the rate based model. In addition to the
tank stages there are also the reboiler and condenser, which are modeled as equilibrium
stages. This gives a total of 32 stages or 62 compartments which must be modeled. Each
stage inside the tank is 20 cm long. Furthermore, a compressor and a throttle valve are
added to change the pressure and temperature between the inner tank and outer shell.
The feed stream contains 95 mol/s water and 5 mol/s ethanol, corresponding to a com-
position of 5 mol% ethanol. The inner tank pressure is set to 0.1 bar and the outer shell
pressure is set to 0.3 bar. An existing model made in Aspen Plus is available and used
for comparison [9]. This model is also based on the nonequilibrium modeling, however,
it is made as an vertical packed column. The diﬀerent orientation and the packing both
have an inﬂuence on the transfer rates. The horizontal tank could be modeled as a packed
column if it is assumed there is suﬃcient mixing in the tank.
The performance criteria for the distillation tank are shown in Table 3.1.
Table 3.1: Performance criteria for the horizontal distillation column
Variable
Performance
criteria
Light product
Ethanol composition (mol%)
≥0.80
Heavy product
Ethanol composition (mol%)
≤0.01
Light product
Flow rate (mol/s)
≥4.0
3.1
Model assumptions
To simplify the problem some model assumptions were made. These assumptions simpli-
ﬁes the problem but do not aﬀect the overall performance of the model.
The compartments inside the tank can be described by the rate based model and the
condenser and reboiler by the equilibrium model. The reboiler and condenser can be
modeled as equilibrium stages by design [18][20].
It is assumed there are no reactions taking place. The vapor phase is assumed to be
an ideal gas. There is mechanical equilibrium so that P V
j
= P L
j = Pj with no pressure
drop. The mass transfer only occurs at the interface, in which case it is assumed that the
transport resistances in the bulk phases are high.
Page 23 of 100
==PAGE:32==
3.2. Procedure for solving of the problem
June, 2018
The system is considered to be a binary system of water and ethanol. This assumption
implies that any solid fermentation residue have been removed before the distillation and
no byproducts was formed.
The feed into the distillation tank is assumed to be pure liquid, so that air and CO2 in
the tank is avoided.
It is also assumed that Wilson’s model for activity coeﬃcients can be used for the liquid
phase, because ethanol is miscible in water and only one liquid phase will be present.
For the dynamic model it is further assumed that the interface is in steady state, with
no holdups of material or energy. This is a valid assumption due to the small interface
thickness [18].
To simplify the geometry it is assumed that the inner tank is half full of liquid. For
the outer shell it is assumed the liquid phase is both at the bottom of tank and as a ﬁlm
around the inner tank wall. For the calculations of the mass and heat transfer coeﬃcients,
the geometry of these two liquid phases are assumed to be one ﬂat plane, which implies
there is no mixing of the phases. Later this assumption will be revisited.
Any heat transfer to and from the surroundings is neglected. For the heat integration the
inner tank wall is assumed to be very thin and that the liquid ﬁlm is on both sides of
the wall. The assumption of liquid ﬁlm on both sides of the wall, is due to heat transfer
between two liquids in general is higher than between vapor and liquid or vapor and vapor.
3.2
Procedure for solving of the problem
During this project a rate based model of the complete horizontal tank with heat inte-
gration will be implemented and solved in Matlab Simulink. After the implementation
various scenarios will be simulated. The modeling and simulation of the horizontal tank
can be quite complex, so to ease the implementation the problem is divided in four steps:
Step I. First step is to set up a rate based model for a single compartment j, without
heat integration, anywhere in the tank, except the reboiler and condenser.
Both a steady steady model is to be made and solved as well as a dynamic
model. An equilibrium model for the condenser and reboiler will also be set
up.
Step II. The next step is to expand the single compartment model to a multiple com-
partment model, both for a steady state and a dynamic case. This multiple
compartment model, along with equilibrium models for the condenser and re-
boiler, will be able to describe the distillation process without the outer shell
Page 24 of 100
==PAGE:33==
3.2. Procedure for solving of the problem
June, 2018
heat integration. When the steady state and dynamic multiple compartment
models are implemented, they are solved.
Step III. Third step is to add the heat transfer across the tank. After this is implemented
in both the multiple compartment steady state and the dynamic models, the
implemented models will be simulated.
Step IV. The last step is to investigate diﬀerent scenarios to the extent time permits.
The scenarios that could be investigated are: Change of the input variables, for
an example if the ethanol concentration in the fermentation liquid is higher
or lower. Adding a third component to the fermentation liquid, this could
be a side product from the fermentation, such as another alcohol or a ester.
Additionally, a control strategy for the dynamic model could be, to ensure
proper separation.
The addition of a third component and the design of a control conﬁguration
are time consuming, therefore, these tasks will only be done if there is time
for it.
Figure 3.2 shows a ﬂowchart of the procedure for the development of the heat integrated
horizontal distillation tank model. A detailed description of each task can be found in
the referenced section, e.g. to see how the single compartment model was implemented
in Matlab Simulink see section 5.
Both steady state and dynamic models were made for all developed models. However,
only the single compartment, the condenser and reboiler and the optimized heat integrated
tank models have been simulated and reported in both steady state and dynamic mode.
The other models are only simulated in steady state mode.

tank, with references to sections where each task is described in details.

4
Model properties
As mentioned in section 2.4 properties and property models are necessary for the solution
of the distillation process models. Furthermore, the geometry relations of the tank is
necessary, e.g. to determine the interfacial area for a ﬂat plane and molar holdups.
4.1
State dependent properties
The ﬁrst state dependent property to be determined is the equilibrium ratio, which de-
pends on the liquid activity coeﬃcient and saturation pressure for each component. The
liquid activity coeﬃcient is found from the Wilson model as described by Taylor [21]. The
Wilson model requires binary interaction parameters, these have been found in the Aspen
documentation [33], the water component is denoted (1) and ethanol (2):
ln Λ12 = −0.0503 −69.6372

The next properties to be determined are the mass transfer rates. These also depend
on the activity coeﬃcients found from Wilson’s model as well as some dimensionless
numbers. Temperature dependent correlations for the liquid and vapor density, viscosity,
thermal conductivity and heat capacity are found on the Thermal Fluid Central website
Page 27 of 100
==PAGE:36==
4.2. Geometry relations
June, 2018
[34]. These correlations are only valid for a certain temperature interval approximately
25 −225 ◦C. The correlations can be found in Appendix A.1.
Constant diﬀusion coeﬃcients are found, independent of temperature. The binary liquid
diﬀusion coeﬃcient for water in ethanol is DL
12 = 0.69 · 10−9 m2/s [35]. For the vapor
phase, the diﬀusion coeﬃcients of water in air has been used, instead of the water in
ethanol vapor diﬀusion coeﬃcient. This is because it is diﬃcult to ﬁnd any data in the
literature on vapor diﬀusion coeﬃcients other than in air. The diﬀusion coeﬃcient of
water in air is DV
12 = 24 · 10−6 m2/s [36]. Because of the diﬃculty of ﬁnding diﬀusion
coeﬃcients, they are assumed to be independent of temperature.
The linear velocities are needed for the calculations of Reynold numbers and concentra-
tions in the vapor and liquid phase. The linear velocities depend on the volumetric and
the molar ﬂow rates. To simplify the two phases can be set to have equal linear velocities.
This also means the two phases have equal residence time in the tank, if the two phase
cross-sectional areas are equal which would be true for a half full tank.
With the diﬀusion coeﬃcients, linear velocities, densities, viscosities, thermal conductiv-
ities and heat capacities for both liquid and vapor phases, the dimensionless numbers
can be found and using those, the mass and energy transfer rates can be determined as
described in section 2.4.
The vapor phase transfer coeﬃcient for ethanol in water and water in ethanol must be
equal, therefore only one component’s properties are necessary. The same applies for the
liquid phase.
For the enthalpies it is assumed that the heat capacities are temperature independent.
The heat capacities and reference enthalpies are found on NIST’s web-book [25]. For
the water the constant heat capacities are CL
P,1 = 75 and CV
P,1 = 27 J/(mol K) and
the reference enthalpy is H1(Tref) = H1(373 K) = 40660 J/mol. For ethanol the heat
capacities are CL
P,2 = 112.4 and CV
P,2 = 87.53 J/(mol K) and the reference enthalpy is
H2(Tref) = H2(352 K) = 38600 J/mol. The phase enthalpies are calculated with these
values from equation (2.4.19).

the corde of the liquid length as in equation (4.2.3) plus the circumference of the liquid
ﬁlm times the stage length.
With all the properties expressed, either as correlations or constants, the model can be
written in Matlab Simulink and solved. The property expressions do not change from
case to case, so they can be used for the simple single compartment model or the complex
fully heat integrated tank model.
5
Single compartment
The ﬁrst step in order to set up a complete model is to model a single compartment in
the inner tank or in the outer shell without the heat integration shown in Figure 3.1. Any
compartment inside the tank can be modeled by using the rate based model, either in
steady state or in a dynamic mode. The reboiler and condenser are modeled as equilibrium
stages separately.
Page 29 of 100
==PAGE:38==

June, 2018
It is decided to implement all the models in Simulink with system functions (s-functions).
The reason for this is it will be easier to set up the complete tank model with multiple
compartments later on.
Furthermore, control conﬁgurations are easier to visualize in
Simulink compared to a Matlab script. The Simulink models use s-functions, these codes
can be found in Appendix A.3.

The dynamic rate based model is a diﬀerential algebraic equation system, and thus the
model can not be solved in the same way as for the steady state models. The Simulink
model set up is shown in Figure 5.2.
Figure 5.2: Simulink model set up for the dynamic rate based model.
In this Simulink model, the algebraic equations and diﬀerential equations are solved si-
multaneously with provided initial values for each time step.
5.3
Test scenarios
With many variables, there are many scenarios which could be tested. Two interesting
scenarios have been chosen, the description of these are shown in Table 5.1
Page 34 of 100
==PAGE:43==
5.4. Simulation results
June, 2018
Table 5.1: Interesting scenarios for test and validation of model.
Variables
Scenario I
“Stripping”
Scenario II
“Rectifying”
Inlet ﬂow rate
Lj−1 > Vj+1
Lj−1 > Vj+1
Inlet liquid
composition
x1,j−1 > x2,j−1
x1,j−1 < x2,j−1
Inlet vapor
composition
y1,j+1 > y2,j+1

Temperature
T V

T V
j+1 > T L
j−1
Pressure
Low pressure
High pressure
These two scenarios are interesting because they represent diﬀerent parts of the tank
distillation. Scenario I in Table 5.1 corresponds to the stripping section of a distillation
column, in this case the inner tank of the horizontal tank. Scenario II corresponds to
the rectifying section of a column, the outer shell in the horizontal tank. In general for
a stripping section the condensation of the heavy compound occurs, and for a rectifying
section the evaporation of the light compound occurs [23].
The results for the simulations in Matlab Simulink can then be compared with results
from an Aspen Plus model. An existing Aspen Plus model have been provided and is
used for all comparisons [9]. The Aspen model is a steady state model for the horizontal
tank. However, Aspen does not provide the option of using a horizontal distillation, so
the model implemented is a pseudo-horizontal distillation model, made as vertical packed
stripping and rectifying columns. The model still uses the rate based model. The diﬀerent
geometry aﬀects the interfacial area and thus the transfer rates, since these depend on
the interfacial area.
The dynamic model can be tested by verifying the system that is in steady state for
the found steady state values. Then the initial values can be changed to investigate the
response, mainly whether the variables will reach steady state or not.
5.4
Simulation results
The steady state and dynamic models have been simulated for the above mentioned
scenarios and compared to values from the Aspen model simulation. The following section
shows the results and discussion from the simulations.

Scenario I: stripping section, steady state
The inlet stream speciﬁcations for scenario I in Table 5.1, are shown in Table 5.2, it is
assumed that there is no feed to the compartment. The values are taken from the existing
Aspen simulation, from the stripping column stage 2. As mentioned previously the Aspen
model, which is used for comparison, is not modelled as a horizontal tank, but as a packed
column. The main diﬀerences are the transfer rate correlations used and the interfacial
areas. Therefore, some deviation of the results from the Aspen results are expected, the
general trends are expected to be the same.

It can be seen that the Matlab model has the same overall trends as the Aspen model,
e.g. a decrease in Vj and an increase in Lj. One large deviation is the molar transfer
rates. The Aspen model gives high transfer rates compared to the Matlab model. This is
most likely because the Aspen model is designed as a packed vertical column, which has
a signiﬁcant higher interfacial area and thus a higher surface area for the mass transfer
to occur. The inner tank in the horizontal tank has no packing and the surface area for
mass transfer is therefore limited to the ﬁlling of the tank as described earlier.
Since the Matlab and Aspen models are not set up identically with regards to the mass
and heat transfer in each compartment it is diﬃcult to compare the two. However, the
results from the Aspen model are still good to use for comparisons with the Matlab model
at a later time, when improvements of the Matlab model are made. Further comparisons
of the single compartments in the Aspen model and the Matlab model are not done in
this project due to time limitations. The results from the Aspen model are also used for
model validation as will be presented later.
Even though the Matlab model has much lower transfer rates than the Aspen model,
Page 37 of 100
==PAGE:46==
5.4. Simulation results
June, 2018
the stripper seems to be working as expected, with a transfer of water in the vapor to
the liquid. Ideally, the ethanol would be transferred from the liquid phase to the vapor
phase as for the Aspen model, although the ethanol transfer rate is smaller than the water
transfer rate, N1,j > N2,j, as expected.

With the steady state values from holdups, along with the steady state values found
before, it is possible to investigate how the dynamic response of the system is, when an
initial value of a variable is diﬀerent from the steady state value. Some interesting cases
of change in initial values are changes in the phase temperatures or phase ﬂow rates.
For the ﬁrst single compartment scenario, described earlier, two simulations with changes
in initial values from the steady state values were run. The ﬁrst simulation was with a
5 % increase in T V
j
and a 5 % decrease in T L
j compared to the steady state values. The
second simulation was with a 5 % increase in Lj and a 5 % decrease in Vj compared to
the steady state values.
Below in Figure 5.3 is shown the liquid, vapor and interface temperatures dependent on
time for a change in initial value of temperature.
Page 40 of 100
==PAGE:49==
5.4. Simulation results
June, 2018
Figure 5.3: Phase temperature dependent on time for a 5 % increase in T V
j
and a 5 %
decrease in T L
j initial values from the steady state value.
All temperatures reach the steady state value within two minutes. The interface tem-
perature also starts from a new initial value, even though this was not altered. This is
because it depends on the two bulk temperatures.
Figure 5.4 shows the ﬂow rates dependencies on time for the second simulation, with
changes in initial values of ﬂow rates.
Page 41 of 100
==PAGE:50==
5.4. Simulation results
June, 2018
Figure 5.4: Liquid and vapor ﬂow rates dependent on time for a 5 % increase in Lj and
a 5 % decrease in Vj initial values from the steady state values.
For this simulation the vapor and liquid ﬂow rates quickly returns to the steady state
values within two minutes.
Since the dynamic model is nonlinear it is diﬃcult to verify that the dynamic responses
above are as expected.
However, when for example the initial vapor temperature is
increased from steady state, the energy holdup derivative becomes negative (equation
(5.2.24)) which in turns means the vapor temperature derivative becomes negative (equa-
tion (5.2.14)) as it can also be seen on Figure 5.3. Similar deductions can be made for
the liquid temperature and the ﬂow rates.
All the 23 variables which are solved for in the dynamic model depend on each other, and
what is not shown in the two ﬁgures above is how other variables change. Any change in
any initial value of the variables might lead to changes in other variables, and possibly
new steady state values. For example: if the liquid ﬂow rate is increased, the total molar
holdup is decreased to account for this and return to steady state. If the energy holdup
is not changed, then the temperature must increase.
Page 42 of 100
==PAGE:51==
6. Model validation
June, 2018
6
Model validation
The results from the model are not necessarily valid, so to ensure the model is ﬁnding the
correct results, some model validating tests are made. The validation tests in this section
are for the thermodynamic model (Wilson) and the transfer rate model, both described
in section 2.4. The Matlab scripts used for the model validation can, like the s-functions,
be found in Appendix A.3.
6.1
Thermodynamic model validation
The thermodynamic model is tested according to the following relations from Elliot [37]:
ln γi = 0
for
xi = 1
(6.1.1)
GE
RT =
X
i
xi ln γi
(6.1.2)
x1 ·

∂xi
!
T,P
+ x2 ·

∂xi
!
T,P
= 0
(6.1.3)
Furthermore, a plot can be made to see if ln γi declines to 0 for xi →1. For a binary system
a total of ﬁve equations and one plot can be used to validate the model. The Gibbs-Duhem
equation (6.1.3) requires the composition derivative of the logarithmic activity coeﬃcient,
these are found by Taylor [21][23]. From the Gibbs-Duhem equation (6.1.3) it further
follows that d ln γi
dxi
→0 for xi →1.
A Matlab script has been made which tests the above relations and returns “1” if they
are true for an input of T I
j , xI
1,j and xI
2,j. These input are found by solving the single
compartment steady state model. The Matlab script for the thermodynamic model test
is made as a function called gamma_test.m.
With the values of T I
j , xI
1,j and xI
2,j from scenario I, section 5.4.1, the thermodynamic
model can be tested as described above. Doing so gives as a result that all three relations,
equations (6.1.1) to (6.1.3), are true. The ln γi vs xi plot is shown in Figure 6.1, where it
can be seen that ln γi →0 for xi →1 and d ln γi
dxi
→0 for xi →1.
Page 43 of 100
==PAGE:52==
6.1. Thermodynamic model validation
June, 2018
Figure 6.1: Logarithmic activity coeﬃcient against liquid interface composition
The same tests was performed for scenario II with the scenario speciﬁcations from section
5.4.2, and once again the four relations, equations (6.1.1)-(6.1.3), are all true. The ln γi-xi
plot for the second scenario is the same as Figure 6.1.
The binary water-ethanol mixture is an azeotropic mixture. The azeotrope is low-boiling
at around 85 mol% ethanol at atmospheric pressure [38]. A simple test of the thermody-
namic model is to plot a T-xy diagram for the binary mixture at a ﬁxed pressure. Figure
6.2 shows such a T-xy diagram for the ethanol composition at 0.1 bar.
Page 44 of 100
==PAGE:53==
6.1. Thermodynamic model validation
June, 2018
Figure 6.2: T-xy diagram for the ethanol - water mixture at 0.1 bar.
As it can be seen from the ﬁgure, the mixture is azeotropic, with a pure water boiling
point at around 319 K and pure ethanol boiling point at around 302 K. The azeotrope is
a low-boiling azeotrope at approximately 85 mol% ethanol. For distillation it would be
diﬃcult to reach the azeotrope composition, and a realistic ethanol product would have
a composition of approximately 80 mol% ethanol at this pressure.
Figure 6.3 shows the T-xy diagram at 1 bar. Here it can be seen that the azeotrope does
depend on pressure, but it does not move much.
Page 45 of 100
==PAGE:54==
6.2. Transfer rate model validation
June, 2018
Figure 6.3: T-xy diagram for the ethanol - water mixture at 1 bar.
From Figure 6.3 it can be seen that the boiling points at 1 bar, Tb(water) ≈373 K and
Tb(ethanol) ≈351 K, are close to being equal to the standard boiling points Tb(water) =
373.15 K and Tb(ethanol) = 351.5 k [25]. The azeotrope is around 85 mol% ethanol at
atmospheric pressure, similar to values found in literature [38].
Azeotropic data on the ethanol - water binary mixture further support the statement that
the azeotrope does not move much with pressure [39, p. 381-383].

example 11.5.2 by Taylor and Krishna [23, p. 287] with values from the Aspen model.

For an equilibrium model there is no mass transfer, and the ﬂuxes would be zero. The mass
ﬂuxes for the above conditions are found in the same way as before to be N1,j = 2.5·10−8
mol/s and N2,j = −2.6 · 10−8 mol/s. Since the molar transfer rates are very close to zero,
the ﬂuxes can be assumed to be zero, and the rate based model can be assumed to be
an equilibrium stage, when the bulk temperatures and compositions for the vapor, liquid
and interface are equal.

By comparing the results with the assumption of ideal liquids, it seems that whether the
correction of the transfer rates are included or not does not change the results signiﬁcantly.
Overall the assumption of ideal liquids gives results from the Matlab model much closer
to the results from the Aspen model, than when the Wilson model was used.
This could mean Aspen has a diﬀerent way of using the Wilson activity coeﬃcient model
than is used in the Matlab model. One likely explanation is that in Aspen there are
fewer assumptions, for example the liquid and vapor ﬁlm might have an inﬂuence on the
transfer rates and therefore the interface heat and mass transfer rates are included in
Aspen (eI
j, N I
i,j). This also means additional equations for the ﬁlms are added, which
adds complexity to the model.
The diﬀerences between the Aspen and the Matlab models will also be discussed later.
7
Condenser and reboiler
Both the reboiler and the condenser are assumed to be equilibrium stages. In this section
the modeling of an equilibrium stage is described for both the steady state and dynamic
cases. The model equations for the equilibrium model is described in section 2.1.
7.1
Steady state equilibrium model
The steady state equilibrium model is solved in the same way as the rate based model, but
since the equilibrium model is a simpler model fewer variables are needed. The variables

in the liquid phase is equal to the temperature in the vapor phase.

8
Multiple compartments
The horizontal tank can now be set up by connecting multiple single compartment rate
based models and equilibrium models for the partial condenser and reboiler.
Page 57 of 100
==PAGE:66==
8.1. Compressor and throttle valve
June, 2018
As speciﬁed earlier, in the problem description (section 3), the tank consists of 30 com-
partments in both the inner tank and the outer shell and a reboiler and a condenser.
Furthermore, the vapor entering the outer shell is compressed by an adiabatic compressor
and the liquid leaving the outer shell is depressurized with a throttle valve. During this
throttling an adiabatic isenthalpic ﬂash occurs.
In this section the modeling of the compressor and throttle valve is ﬁrst described, then
the set up of the complete tank is described. Initially the heat transfer between the inner
tank and outer shell is omitted to simplify the problem. This means the distillation is
an adiabatic process. Later the heat transfer will be added and the performance will be
compared with that of the adiabatic distillation.
8.1
Compressor and throttle valve
Both the compressor and throttle valve are considered to be adiabatic processes. This
means for the compression the temperature increases and for the throttling the tempera-
ture decreases with the pressure. Both are assumed to only operate in steady state.
8.1.1
Adiabatic compression
The temperature after an isentropic adiabatic compression is described by Biegler et al.
[41]:
T2 = T1
P2
P1
 γ−1
γ
η
(8.1.1)
Where T1 and P1 are the temperature and pressure before the compression and T2 and
P2 after the compression. The heat capacity ratio, γ = Cp
Cv , is equal to 1.4 for an ideal gas
and the compressor eﬃciency is typically set to be 80 % (η = 0.80) [41].
The compressor is implemented in Simulink by taking the vapor ﬂow temperature and
multiply it by the constant factor

P2
P1
 γ−1
γ η =

0.3
0.1
 1.4−1
1.4 · 0.80 = 1.095.
It is assumed the compressor is perfectly controlled and thus there is no need to deﬁne
the compressor duty.
8.1.2
Adiabatic ﬂash
The throttling is described by an adiabatic ﬂash. The results from an adiabatic ﬂash is
calculated by following the Rachford-Rice procedure [15]. The Rachford-Rice procedure is
a set of nonlinear equations which can be solved using Newton’s method. The equations
Page 58 of 100
==PAGE:67==
8.2. Adiabatic distillation
June, 2018
for the adiabatic ﬂash are as follows [15]:
V −Fβ = 0
(8.1.2)
xi −
zi
1 + β(Ki −1) = 0
for
i = 1, 2, ..., C
(8.1.3)
yi −Kixi = 0
for
i = 1, 2, ..., C
(8.1.4)
F −L −V = 0
(8.1.5)
βHV + (1 −β)HL −HF = 0
(8.1.6)
Where zi is the feed composition and the equilibrium ratio is found with the Wilson
activity coeﬃcient model as described earlier. The vapor ﬂow to feed ﬂow ratio, β = V
F is
found by solving the equation below [15]:
C
X
i=1
zi(1 −Ki)
1 + β(Ki −1) = 0
(8.1.7)
With two components a total of seven equation is solved for seven variables (L, V, T, xi, yi).
The throttle valve is implemented in Simulink by implementing the adiabatic ﬂash model
equations above in a s-function, throt_sfun.m, with a feed input and a speciﬁed outlet
pressure. The adiabatic ﬂash is then solved iteratively in a similar fashion as previously
shown with an Algebraic Constraint block in Simulink.
8.2
Adiabatic distillation
As mentioned previously the distillation process is initially assumed to be adiabatic. First
the steady state adiabatic tank model was built, and subsequently the dynamic model.
The complete tank model was implemented in Simulink by ﬁrst connecting 30 steady
state rate based compartment models. These compartments deﬁne the inner tank with a
pressure of 0.1 bar and geometry corresponding to the inner tank. Stage 32 was added
as a reboiler with liquid input from inner tank compartment 31 and the vapor boilup is
returned to inner tank compartment 31 in counter current. The liquid leaving the reboiler
is removed as the heavy key product. Compartment 2 in the inner tank is also the feed
stage, so a liquid feed consisting of 95 mol/s water and 5 mol/s ethanol is added. The
vapor leaving compartment 2 in the inner tank is then sent through the compressor and
the temperature of this is thus increased as described earlier (see Figure 2.2).
The high temperature vapor is then sent into compartment 31 in the outer shell, and is
sent through another 30 steady state rate based model compartments in the outer shell,
with a pressure of 0.3 bar and geometry of the outer shell, until it enters the partial
condenser in stage 1. The condenser is modeled as an equilibrium stage. The liquid that
Page 59 of 100
==PAGE:68==
8.3. Steady state simulation
June, 2018
is condensed is sent back into compartment 2 in the outer shell and the vapor leaving the
condenser is removed as the light key product. The liquid from the condenser ﬂows from
compartment 2 to 31 in the outer shell, after which it is throttled as described earlier,
and then returned to compartment 2 in the inner tank.
The general conﬁguration of the complete horizontal tank model in Simulink is shown in
Appendix A.2.
In this conﬁguration the light key product is removed as vapor, however the Simulink
model is set up to allow for a fraction of the liquid to be removed as well. This is done to
make the model as general as possible.
For every compartment in the model the values of the output variables are logged, so they
can be used as initial guess of the dynamic model and in order to show the tank proﬁles.
The dynamic model is set up similarly to the steady state model. Again the dynamic
model is ﬁrst assumed to be at steady state, so the steady state values of the holdups can
be found and used for the dynamic model as initial values. Dynamic simulations are only
done for the heat integrated model and not the adiabatic model, even though dynamic
models were set up for all models.
One problem with the steady state model is that every compartment needs an initial guess
of the output variables so the Simulink solver can ﬁnd a solution. The s-functions used
do not allow for guesses of the compositions to be negative, so if poor initial guesses are
used the simulation might stop prematurely with an error.
After the model was set up in Simulink the reboiler and condenser duties were determined.
At ﬁrst it was considered to estimate the duties by using the graphical McCabe-Thiele
method, however due to the limitations of this method it was decided to ﬁnd the duties
by trial-and-error instead. When trying to ﬁnd the heat duties it soon became apparent
that the adiabatic ﬂash in the throttle valve caused trouble. One of the major issues is
that a signiﬁcant amount of vapor is formed after the throttling. This vapor is sent back
in to the outer shell and thus increases the total molar ﬂow in the outer shell. With a high
molar ﬂow it follows that the condenser duty is also high. The high internal molar ﬂow
was later reduced by introducing the heat integration between the inner tank and outer
shell. Even with the reduction of internal ﬂow rates in the tank, the reﬂux and boilup
ratios were still high. As it will be discussed later this is connected to the mass transfer
in the tank.
8.3
Steady state simulation
Due to the issues with the need of suitable initial guesses and the high molar ﬂow in the
outer shell it proved diﬃcult to ﬁnd heat duties of the reboiler and condenser which gave
Page 60 of 100
==PAGE:69==
8.3. Steady state simulation
June, 2018

the reboiler and condenser duties and other speciﬁcations for the adiabatic tank model
as shown in Table 8.1.

Page 61 of 100
==PAGE:70==
9. Multiple compartments with mixing
June, 2018
With this design of the adiabatic tank it is possible to separate the fermentation liquid.
However, it requires high heat duty in the condenser and high molar ﬂows in the outer
shell. Furthermore, the separation is not satisfying the performance criteria in Table 3.1,
since the molar composition of ethanol in the light product is far from the azeotrope at
approximately 85 mol%, and the water composition in the heavy product is not above 99
mol% which would be more preferable. It is therefore decided to change the internal ﬂow
inside the tank, in order to improve the separation performance. The vapor and liquid
phases are from now on assumed to be mixed, which highly increases the interfacial areas
in both the inner tank and outer shell and thus the mass transfer is increased.
9
Multiple compartments with mixing
In an attempt to improve the adiabatic distillation it is investigated whether the internal
ﬂow can be changed to improve the separation. It is decided to assume the mass transfer
coeﬃcients can be found from the correlations described by Bravo and Fair [23] and
the heat transfer coeﬃcients can be determined from the correlations by Chilton and
Colburn [15]. These correlations are originally developed for randomly packed distillation
columns, so when using them in the horizontal tank it is assumed that the vapor and
liquid interfaces can be realized as a packed column. The packed column design is what
has also been used in the aforementioned Aspen model [9].
The ﬂow can be assumed to behave like a randomly packed column if the phases are mixed.
This means there are some liquid droplets in the vapor phase and some vapor bubbles in
the liquid phase. This is physically possible with a mechanical mixer or spray nozzles,
however, the explicit treatment of any mixing equipment is omitted in this project.
Figure 9.1 shows how the mixing of the phases results in droplets and bubbles in the tank,
which in turn increases the interfacial areas.
Figure 9.1: Horizontal tank with mixing of liquid and vapor phases. The grey hatched
areas are the liquid phase.
Page 62 of 100
==PAGE:71==
9.1. Packed column mass and heat transfer coeﬃcient correlations
June, 2018
9.1
Packed column mass and heat transfer coeﬃcient correla-
tions
The binary mass and heat transfer coeﬃcients are no longer found as described in section
2.4, but by using the Bravo-Fair and the Chilton-Colburn correlations [15][23].
The Bravo and Fair correlations for binary mass transfer coeﬃcients in randomly packed
columns are [23]:
kV = apDV
ABA(ReV )0.7(ScV )0.333(apdp)−2
(9.1.1)
kL = 0.0051(ReL)0.667(ScL)−0.5(apdp)0.4

µLg
!−0.333
(9.1.2)
Where ap is the phase contact area, here set to be ap = 50 m2/m3, dp is the characteristic
length of the packing material, here set to be 1 mm and A is a factor which is equal to 2
if dp < 12 mm. The gravitational acceleration is g = 9.81 m/s2.
The binary heat transfer coeﬃcients are found from Chilton-Colburn correlations [15]:
hV = kV ρV CV
p (LeV )2/3
(9.1.3)
hL = kLρLCL
p (LeL)1/2
(9.1.4)
Where the Lewis number is deﬁned as Le = Sc
Pr.
The interfacial areas are estimated by ﬁnding the volume of a single compartment in the
inner tank (IT) and the outer shell (OS) and multiply it by the phase contact area. These
interfacial areas are empirical guesses based on the volume of a single compartment.
V olIT = ac,IT · L
N = 2.545 · 6
30 m3/compartment = 0.509 m3/compartment

(9.1.8)
These interfacial areas take account of the planar liquid-vapor phase interface, which was
used before, and the droplets and bubbles present in the two phases.
Later it was found that the interfacial area in the outer shell needs to be four times the
interfacial area in the inner tank, aI
OS = 101.80 m2/compartment, in order to ensure a
high ethanol purity in the light key product. Physically this means there are more bubbles
and droplets in the outer shell compared to the inner tank. The inner tank interfacial
area could also be increased to the same value, however, it was found that it did not have
any signiﬁcant eﬀect on the product streams as it will be shown later.

These correlations are implemented in the rate based single compartment s-functions
from before and replace the previous mass and heat transfer coeﬃcient correlations. The
changed code can be found in Appendix A.3. In the rate based model s-functions both
correlations for ﬂat plane geometry and randomly packed columns are implemented, with
the option of comment out the correlations that are not used.

9.3
Comparison to Aspen model
The model from Aspen Plus which was used earlier for comparison had diﬀerent heat
duties for the reboiler and condenser and diﬀerent interfacial areas [9]. It was considered
to use the values from Aspen in the Simulink model, however, by doing so the performance
criteria in Table 3.1 would not be satisﬁed.
An adiabatic Aspen model was made for comparison by setting the stage duties to zero
in the heat integrated Aspen model, and thus remove the heat transfer from the outer
shell to the inner tank.
If, for example, only the interfacial areas were changed to that of the Aspen model,
Page 66 of 100
==PAGE:75==
10. Heat integrated multiple compartments
June, 2018
aI = 17.672 m2/stage and aI = 31.416 m2/stage for the inner tank and the outer shell
respectively, and the heat duties kept as shown in Table 9.1 the light product ethanol
composition was lowered to yEtOH = 71.8 mol%. The condenser duty from Aspen is lower
than the one used in Simulink, so if this also was changed, the ethanol composition in the
light product would decrease further. It is therefore not beneﬁcial to use all the values
from Aspen as inputs to the Simulink model.
A key diﬀerence between the Aspen Plus model and the Matlab Simulink model is that
Aspen have all model equations, parameters and transfer rate correlations implemented,
while they were explicitly written in Matlab. Because some of the parameters and cor-
relations in the Aspen model were not readily available to extract, these were found in
various literature sources for the Matlab model.
Because of the diﬀerence between the Aspen and Simulink models it was decided, due to
limited time for the project, to not further compare the two models, but instead continue
to work on making a dynamic model for the heat integrated horizontal distillation vessel
for fermentation liquids. At a later time eﬀorts should be made to make comparable
models by changing some of the correlations and assumptions in the Simulink model.
10
Heat integrated multiple compartments
With the adiabatic tank model running, and the assumption of packed column ﬂow be-
haviour, it was found that the ethanol and water in the fermentation liquid feed could
be separated to a satisfactory degree. Now the tank is heat integrated by adding energy
transfer across the inner tank wall between the inner tank and the outer shell. When the
heat integration is added and the diabatic tank model is running it can then be compared
to the adiabatic model and attempts to lower the condenser and reboiler duties can be
made.
10.1
Implementation of heat integration
The heat integration is implemented in the model with the heat exchanger design equation
[41]:
Qj = U HIAHI
j ∆T L
j
(10.1.1)
Where U HI is the overall heat transfer coeﬃcient in W/(m2 K) and determines how much
heat can be transfer between two phases. The transfer area AHI
j
is determined by the
contact area between the phases.
It is assumed that there is a liquid ﬁlm on both sides of the inner tank wall, and that
the wall thickness can be neglected. This means that it is a liquid - liquid heat transfer.
Page 67 of 100
==PAGE:76==
10.2. Steady state simulation
June, 2018
Furthermore, it is assumed that the liquid ﬁlm mainly contains water on both sides. With
these assumptions the overall heat transfer coeﬃcient is found in Biegler et al. [41] to be
U HI = 1277.775 W/(m2 K) for liquid water on both tube and shell side. The overall heat
transfer coeﬃcient used in the Aspen model was U HI = 1000 W/(m2 K) [9].
The transfer contact area is the circumference of the inner tank multiplied with the length.
With the 30 compartments the contact area is AHI
j
= π · 1.8 ·
6
30 m2/compartment =
1.131 m2/compartment.
The temperature diﬀerence is between the outer shell compartment liquid temperature
and the inner tank compartment liquid temperature, ∆T L
j = T L
j,OS −T L
j,IT.
The energy that is transferred in a compartment Qj is added to the inner tank and
removed from the outer shell. This heat integration is added in the Simulink models, by
taking the temperature outputs for a compartment from both the inner tank and outer
shell and calculate the energy from equation (10.1.1).
This energy is then sent in to
the compartment model, with a negative sign for the inner tank and a positive sign for
the outer shell to ensure energy is added to the inner tank and removed from the outer
compartment.
The implementation of this heat integration between compartments in Simulink is shown
in Appendix A.2.

The same trends as for the adiabatic tank model proﬁles (Figure 9.2 and 9.3) can be seen
