Figure 11.3:
Energy transfer between the outer shell and inner tank proﬁle in the
optimized diabatic tank for each double layer theoretical stage. Note the feed is sent to
the inner tank at length = 0 m.
The energy transfer is high at the ends of the tank, since this is where the highest tem-
perature diﬀerence is, which in turn increases the energy according to equation (10.1.1).
The reason for the larger temperature diﬀerences between the outer shell and inner tank
liquids, which can also be seen on Figure 11.1 (top), is due to the changes in ﬂow behav-
ior. These changes occur when feed is added, when the vapor is compressed, the liquid is
throttled, vapor is sent back from the reboiler or liquid is returned from the condenser.
11.2
Steady state models comparison
Four diﬀerent models have been developed. First the adiabatic model with the assump-
tion of ﬂat plane geometry (adiabatic model no. 1), then the adiabatic model with the
assumption of packed column geometry (adiabatic model no. 2), then the diabatic model
Page 72 of 100
==PAGE:81==
11.2. Steady state models comparison
June, 2018
with the same duties as the adiabatic model (diabatic model no. 1) and ﬁnally the opti-
mized diabatic model (diabatic model no. 2). In Figure 11.4 the total duty for reboiler
and condenser and the ethanol composition in the light product are compared for the four
diﬀerent models.
Figure 11.4: Comparison of the total duties and ethanol composition in light products
for the four developed steady state models. Adiabatic 1 is the model described in section
8, adiabatic 2 is the model described in section 9, diabatic 1 is the model described in
section 10 and diabatic 2 is the model described in section 11.
The ﬁgure above shows that the ﬁrst model developed, with no mixing of the two phases,
had a high energy requirement and a low product purity. When the phases were assumed
to be mixed in the second model, it was possible to lower the energy requirement and
increase the product purity. The third model developed, with heat integration, had the
same energy requirements as the second model but a lower light product purity due to a
lower temperature in the outer shell. The heavy product purity on the other hand was
increased, which is not shown here. The last optimized diabatic model had an even lower
energy requirement while still maintaining a high product purity.
To sum up the comparison of the four diﬀerent developed steady state model, it can be
concluded that to have a satisfactory degree of separation, the tank must be described as
a packed column so that the phases are mixed. Furthermore, with the heat integration
it is possible to save a lot of energy to the condenser and reboiler without lowering the
product purity signiﬁcantly.
Page 73 of 100
==PAGE:82==
11.3. Sensitivity analysis
June, 2018
11.3
Sensitivity analysis
The heat integrated horizontal distillation tank model is a complex model, and because of
various model simpliﬁcations and assumptions there are some uncertainties in the results.
Therefore, a sensitivity analysis is carried out by investigating the eﬀect of changing some
of the more uncertain variables. The variables which are investigated are shown below:
• Transfer rate coeﬃcients (kL, kV , hL, hV )
• Overall heat transfer coeﬃcient (U HI)
• Interfacial areas (aI
IT, aI
OS)
The variables are changed by multiplying with a sensitivity factor called fSA, run a steady
state simulation and note the product streams’ ﬂow rates and ethanol compositions. This
factor, fSA, is set to be between 0 and 200 % of the steady state value from the simulations
in section 11.1.
It was decided to change the vapor and liquid mass transfer coeﬃcient at the same time.
Likewise, for the heat transfer coeﬃcients.
A total of 5 diﬀerent sensitivity analysis were made with the sensitivity factor:
1. fSA · kL and fSA · kV
2. fSA · hL and fSA · hV
3. fSA · U HI
4. fSA · aI
IT
5. fSA · aI
OS
To evaluate the results Figures 11.5 and 11.6 show the changes in heavy and light product
ﬂow rates and Figures 11.7 and 11.8 the ethanol compositions in heavy and light products,
for changes in fSA in percentage relative to the steady state value.
Page 74 of 100
==PAGE:83==
11.3. Sensitivity analysis
June, 2018
Figure 11.5: Eﬀect of changes in variables on heavy product ﬂow rate.
Figure 11.6: Eﬀect of changes in variables on light product ﬂow rate.
Based on the two ﬁgures above, which show the product ﬂow rates dependencies on
changes in fSA, it can be seen that increases in the variables does not have a high eﬀect
Page 75 of 100
==PAGE:84==
11.3. Sensitivity analysis
June, 2018
compared to decreases in the variables. It is mainly the mass transfer coeﬃcients and
outer shell interfacial area which have an eﬀect on the ﬂow rates.
The heat transfer
coeﬃcients and the overall heat transfer coeﬃcient also have noticeable eﬀects, while the
inner tank interfacial area have almost no eﬀect.
Figure 11.7: Eﬀect of changes in variables on heavy product ethanol composition.
Page 76 of 100
==PAGE:85==
11.3. Sensitivity analysis
June, 2018
Figure 11.8: Eﬀect of changes in variables on light product ethanol composition.
The ethanol composition in the heavy product stream, Figure 11.7, is largely aﬀected by
the heat transfer coeﬃcients and the overall heat transfer coeﬃcients, and only to some
extent depend on the mass transfer coeﬃcients and outer shell interfacial area. For the
ethanol composition in the light product it is only the mass transfer coeﬃcients and outer
shell interfacial area which have a signiﬁcant eﬀect. Again it seems that the increases in
fSA have a smaller eﬀect than decreases.
When the overall heat transfer coeﬃcient is increased the ethanol composition decreases
in the heavy and light products. This is because with a higher U HI the liquid temperature
in the inner tank is increased, while it is decreased in the outer shell. This means a higher
degree of ethanol evaporation occurs in the inner tank, but a lower degree in the outer
shell.
From the sensitivity analysis it can be concluded that changes in either the mass, heat,
overall heat transfer coeﬃcients or the outer shell interfacial area will have a signiﬁcant
eﬀect on the product streams, with regards to ﬂow rate and composition. Furthermore,
to ensure a high ethanol content in the light product it is paramount that the outer
shell interfacial area is kept high. The reason for the outer shell interfacial area being
so important, compared to the inner tank interfacial area, is because most of rectifying
of the ethanol in the light product occurs in the outer shell (see composition proﬁles in
Figure 11.2).
The transfer coeﬃcients would be diﬃcult to change, since these depend on many diﬀerent
Page 77 of 100
==PAGE:86==
11.4. Dynamic simulations
June, 2018
parameters. The interfacial areas on the other hand is mostly aﬀected by the degree of
mixing of the liquid and vapor phases. A higher degree of mixing would increase the
number of bubbles and droplets and thus the interfacial area.
Papadakis et al. have previously made a sensitivity analysis for the heat integration hori-
zontal distillation system [42]. This sensitivity analysis was made based on the previously
mentioned Aspen model [9]. They also found that the interfacial areas and mass transfer
rate coeﬃcients were the variables which were more sensitive to a change compared to
the heat transfer rate coeﬃcients.
11.4
Dynamic simulations
Before the dynamic simulations are shown it should be mentioned that the dynamic
simulations of the multiple compartment models take a long time to compute. This is
due to the algebraic equations that needs to be solved by an iterative method for every
time step. Attempts were made to shorten the simulation time, such as decrease the order
of the diﬀerential solver (ODE15s), set the Simulink model mode to “accelerator mode”
and only compute the sparse Jacobian matrices for each time step. Furthermore a high
relative tolerance was used (1 · 10−3).
The ﬁrst dynamic simulation was a simulation of the system with the steady state values
as initial conditions. This was done to ensure the system was in steady state, which it
was. The results of this are not shown.
The dynamic cases which have been investigated are changes in the molar composition of
ethanol in the feed of +20% corresponding to a ethanol composition of 6 mol%, and a 5
% increase in total feed ﬂow rate.
First the 20 % increase in ethanol feed ﬂow is investigated. This corresponds to a feed
ﬂow rate of 100 mol/s with 6 mol% ethanol and 94 mol% water. Figures 11.9 to 11.11
shows the product molar ﬂow rates, temperatures and compositions respectively.
Page 78 of 100
==PAGE:87==
11.4. Dynamic simulations
June, 2018
Figure 11.9: Heavy (top) and light (bottom) product ﬂow rates for a 20 % increase in
ethanol feed ﬂow.
Figure 11.10: Product temperatures for a 20 % increase in ethanol feed ﬂow.
Page 79 of 100
==PAGE:88==
11.4. Dynamic simulations
June, 2018
Figure 11.11: Heavy product water composition and light product ethanol composition
for a 20 % increase in ethanol feed ﬂow.
It can be seen that the heavy product is hardly aﬀected, which is as expected, since most
of the ethanol is sent to the outer shell and the condenser.
The light product on the other hand is highly aﬀected. The light product molar ﬂow
rate, temperature and ethanol composition all follow the same trend with an initial small
increase and then steep decrease. The initial increase in ethanol composition in light
product is expected, since more ethanol into the tank must also mean more ethanol out.
The steep decrease was also found in the outer shell compartment 2 (the compartment
connected to the condenser). If the vapor ﬂow rate from the second outer shell compart-
ment is decreased, then less vapor is sent to the condenser. This means a decrease in the
light product and reﬂux streams. Since the condenser has a constant duty it also means
the temperature of these streams are reduced.
The vapor ﬂow from the second outer shell compartment was decreased, while the liquid
ﬂow from the second outer shell compartment was increased. This was explained by an
increase in the molar transfer rate of both ethanol and water from the vapor to the liquid.
The increase in transfer rates was in turn connected to an increase in the interfacial tem-
perature in the second outer shell compartment. The interfacial temperature is dependent
on the bulk and interfacial compositions and temperatures and the concentrations of the
vapor and liquid phases. Therefore, there are many possible explanations for this steep
decrease, and it is diﬃcult to pinpoint the exact cause for the decrease.
Page 80 of 100
==PAGE:89==
11.4. Dynamic simulations
June, 2018
For the next simulation a 5 % increase in the total feed ﬂow rate is investigated, but
keeping the feed composition of 5 mol% ethanol and 95 % water. Figures 11.12 to 11.14
shows the product molar ﬂow rates, temperatures and compositions respectively.
Figure 11.12: Heavy (top) and light (bottom) product ﬂow rates for a 5 % increase in
total feed ﬂow.

==PAGE:90==
11.4. Dynamic simulations
June, 2018
Figure 11.13: Product temperatures for a 5 % increase in total feed ﬂow.
Figure 11.14: Heavy product water composition and light product ethanol composition
for a 5 % increase in total feed ﬂow.
It is again the light product that is aﬀected, while the heavy product is stable. The molar
Page 82 of 100
==PAGE:91==
11.4. Dynamic simulations
June, 2018
ﬂow rate is increased as expected and with it the temperature and ﬂow rate. What is not
expected is that the ethanol composition in the light product exceeds the azeotrope and
even becomes higher than 1. A molar composition greater than 1 is physically impossible.
The impossible ethanol composition in the light product, where the azeotrope is crossed,
is likely a numerical error which occurs when the model is solved in Matlab, since Matlab
does not take the azeotrope into account.
The steep decreases for the light product,
which is seen in Figures 11.9 - 11.11, could also be due to a numerical error caused by the
tolerance, this is, however, unlikely. Another reason could be the change from a rate based
model compartment to an equilibrium stage model (second outer shell compartment to
condenser), however, since there are no similar issues with the reboiler this is also unlikely.
It was found the unstable dynamic response for both cases above was directly linked to a
decrease in the condenser holdup. This means that for the change in ethanol content in
the feed and the change in feed ﬂow rate the holdup in the condenser starts to decrease,
which in turn causes the outputs to drift and thus the system becomes unstable. It is
therefore important to keep the holdup in the condenser from declining, for example by
implementing a control strategy. Because of all these unstable responses to a change in
the feed, it is clear that a control conﬁguration is necessary, especially for the condenser
outputs, to avoid drifting nodes.
Smaller disturbances in the feed composition and ﬂow rate were also investigated in order
to see how sensitive the system is to a change from steady state. Therefore, a +5% change
in feed ethanol composition, corresponding to 5.25 mol% ethanol in the feed, and a +1%
in feed ﬂow rate change in total feed ﬂow rate, corresponding to a ﬂow rate of 101 mol/s,
were investigated, too.
For the +5% change in feed ethanol composition the heavy product water composition
and light product ethanol composition are shown in Figure 11.15.

for a 5 % increase in ethanol feed ﬂow.
The trend for a 5 % increase in feed ethanol composition is the same as for a 20 % increase
(see Figure 11.11) only with a reduced magnitude. The ethanol composition in the light
product increases a little and then decreases.

12. Discussion
June, 2018
Figure 11.16: Heavy product water composition and light product ethanol composition
for a 1 % increase in total feed ﬂow.
Again the trends for a 1 % and 5 % (Figure 11.14) increase are the same with an increase
in ethanol composition in the light product, which even crosses the azeotrope.
A small disturbance still destabilizes the system, which further emphasizes the need for
a control conﬁguration.
12
Discussion
In this section a strategy for the implementation of a control conﬁguration is given.
Furthermore, three subjects are discussed. These subjects are the model assumptions,
the diﬀerence between Aspen Plus and Matlab Simulink models and ﬁnally the solving of
the dynamic model.
12.1
Control design strategy
In this subsection it is shortly discussed how a control conﬁguration could be applied to
the horizontal distillation tank. The implementation and tuning of a control conﬁguration
is a complex task and because of the limited time frame this is not done here. However,
general ideas and thoughts for the control strategy are given.
Page 85 of 100
==PAGE:94==
12.1. Control design strategy
June, 2018
Bisgaard et al. have already made a control conﬁguration, based on the top-down bottom-
up analysis, for a concentric heat-integrated distillation column (HIDiC) [30]. The HIDiC
and the horizontal tank described in this thesis have many similarities and it is therefore
plausible that the control conﬁguration described by Bisgaard et al. could be applied, at
least to some extent, to the horizontal tank.
12.1.1
Top-down analysis
As mentioned previously Larsson and Skogestad have presented a top-down bottom-up
procedure for a plantwide control design [29]. This procedure is described below for the
horizontal diabatic tank.
The ﬁrst step of the top-down analysis is to ﬁnd the steady state degrees of freedom. This
is done in order to ﬁnd the optimal steady state operation. This requires the number of
actuators to be known. If it is assumed that the horizontal tank is similar to the HIDiC
there are seven actuators placed upon: the product streams, utility streams for reboiler
(steam) and condenser (cooling water), the condenser reﬂux stream, the throttle valve and
the compressor [30]. The ﬂow rate of the utility streams directly determine the duties of
the condenser and reboiler. Figure 12.1 shows the placement of the valves, including the
throughput manipulator.
Figure 12.1: Horizontal with places actuators including the throughput manipulator.
With two outputs with no steady state eﬀect (condenser and reboiler holdups) the degrees
of freedom are:
DOFSS = Nactuators −Noutputs,0 −Ninputs,0 = 7 −2 −0 = 5
(12.1.1)
If the condenser and reboiler holdups are decided to be paired with the actuators on the
product streams, the ﬁve actuators on the utility streams can be paired freely with ﬁve
steady state degrees of freedom. The ﬁve steady state DOFSS that are selected as inputs
Page 86 of 100
==PAGE:95==
12.1. Control design strategy
June, 2018
for the optimization are the compression ratio, inner tank pressure, reﬂux rate, reboiler
duty (or boilup rate) and ﬂow rate through the throttle valve (TV-1).
The next step is to set up an optimization problem to ﬁnd the optimal steady state
operational point. The objective function J is deﬁned as the cost of feed and utilities
minus the cost of products. The cost of feed and products depend on the composition.
min J = Feed + Utilities - Products
(12.1.2)
s.t.
Constraints on product speciﬁcations
Physical constraints on equipment
Energy constraints on duties
Since ﬁve DOFSS were identiﬁed ﬁve primary controlled variables should also be selected.
Without going into too much details, it would be expected the optimal operating point
would be with a low utility consumption, high purity products and/or high product
ﬂow rates. It is diﬃcult to predict what the exact optimal operational point is, but it
is generally recommended to control the active constraints [31]. Ideas for the primary
controlled variables, CV1, are the reﬂux and boilup ﬂow rates, inner tank pressure, ﬂow
rate through throttle valve and compression ratio. The compositions could also have been
chosen. However, the molar compositions are often diﬃcult to measure compared to ﬂow
rates, so it might be an idea to choose the reﬂux and boilup rates instead.
Final step of the top-down analysis is to decide upon a throughput manipulator (TPM)
location. In this case it is decided to do as Bisgaard et al. and place it on the feed
stream as shown in Figure 12.1. Whether this is the optimal location for maximizing the
throughput is diﬃcult to predict, but in general it should be placed at a bottleneck [31].
12.1.2
Bottom-up design
First step of the bottom-up design is to design the regulatory layer. This is the stabilizing
control layer that is implemented to avoid drifting of the system. The regulatory layer is
made by identifying suitable secondary controlled variables, CV2.
These controlled variables should then be paired with an actuator and by following the
procedure by Bisgaard et al. the pairings could be as follows:
• The light product ﬂow rate (V-1) controls the condenser holdup.
• The heavy product ﬂow rate (V-2) controls the reboiler holdup.
• Condenser duty (V-3) controls the temperature proﬁle, either in the inner tank or
Page 87 of 100
==PAGE:96==
12.2. Model assumptions
June, 2018
the outer shell.
• Reboiler duty (V-4) controls the pressure in the inner tank.
• Condenser reﬂux is controlled by V-5.
• Flow through throttle valve (TV-1) controls the liquid holdup in the outer shell.
• Compressor (C-1) controls the outer shell pressure.
The ﬁrst objective for a control design implementation is to make a simple regulatory
layer with PI-controllers with single input - single output (SISO). This should be done
ﬁrst in order to see if and how the system can be kept from drifting when a disturbance
is imposed. Later the supervisory layer can be added to change the set points and a real
time optimization layer to ﬁnd the optimal set points [29].
As mentioned previously, it is very important to implement a control conﬁguration to
avoid drifting of the outputs. It was especially in the condenser that the outputs drifted.
With proper control of the liquid holdup in the condenser and the reﬂux stream it should
be possible to avoid drifting.
12.2
Model assumptions
In order to simplify the problem several assumptions have been made. The more impor-
tant assumptions include the correlations used for transfer rates, the assumption of some
constant variables (e.g. interfacial areas) and some of the design decisions (e.g. condenser
and reboiler sizes).
As shown previously the transfer rates and interfacial areas have a signiﬁcant eﬀect on the
separation of ethanol from water. It is also the transfer rates which are the most diﬃcult
to predict for the horizontal set up, since no correlations are available.
The interfacial areas are also diﬃcult to predict. Here they were chosen in order to achieve
a high degree of separation. The interfacial area can physically be increased or decreased
by adjusting the mixing of the liquid and vapor phase. However, if the interfacial area
is increased too much, and thus many very small droplets are formed and present in the
vapor, there is a possibility of entrainment of droplets in the vapor and the two phases
would not be in counter-current.
Another assumption is the condenser and reboiler design. The design of these do not
aﬀect the heat duties, but it does aﬀect the holdups. If a control conﬁguration is made to
control the holdups in condenser and reboiler the tuning of the controllers would depend
on the condenser and reboiler design.
Page 88 of 100
==PAGE:97==
12.3. Comparison of Aspen Plus and Matlab Simulink models
June, 2018
It was diﬃcult to ﬁnd the optimal heat duties for condenser and reboiler, since the model
required good initial guesses or the solving would stop prematurely with an error, and
changing the duties largely aﬀected the internal tank ﬂow rates and compositions. Ideally
the duties should be chosen to maximize the product compositions and keep a sensible
reﬂux ratio, but because of these issues with the need of good initial guesses the duties
were found by trial-and-error.
The need of good initial guesses also means the model is not very robust.
To improve the model and to make proper design decisions it would be a good idea
to conduct experiments. In order to do any experiments, the construction of a diabatic
horizontal tank is necessary. Dependent on the goal of the experiments it might be possible
to use a small scale set up which would be cheaper than building the complete diabatic
horizontal tank. Experimental work will also make it possible to conﬁrm, improve or
completely dismiss the developed model.
If the model is to be compared with experiments it is important to keep the assumptions
and conditions as close as possible. This means that the same feed speciﬁcations should
be used, same pressures, have no heat transfer to the surroundings and so on.
Another issue with the developed model was the high boilup and reﬂux ratios. The reason
for these high ratios are most likely the mass transfer rates in the tank. If the transfer
rates in the tank could be increased, the boilup/reﬂux ratios could be lowered.
12.3
Comparison of Aspen Plus and Matlab Simulink models
As mentioned previously an Aspen Plus model is made which could be used for compari-
son. However, as it turned out there was a signiﬁcant diﬀerence in the results of the two
models. The two models are compared below.

As written above a large part of the explanation of the diﬀerence between the two models
is the model implementation. While in Aspen the model equations and state dependent
correlations are all implemented with a large database for constants and correlations it
is written explicitly in Matlab. This makes it diﬃcult to compare the two models, but
since the rate based model is used for both the implementation in Matlab and Aspen it is
unlikely that there are any diﬀerences. One possibility is that Aspen Plus does not have
the assumption of very thin ﬁlm around the interface which would mean more equations
are added to describe the liquid and vapor ﬁlms.
The mass transfer coeﬃcient correlations used in the Aspen Plus model was a Bravo and
Fair correlation for structured packing and not the Bravo and Fair correlation for random
packing used in the Matlab Simulink model. The reason the correlation for random packed
columns were used for the Simulink is that it would be diﬃcult to predict a structured
pattern when the liquid and vapor phases are mixed in the tank.
The state dependent variables on the other hand are not all the same in Matlab as
Aspen, since it proved diﬃcult to ﬁnd all necessary correlations in Aspen some were
found elsewhere in other literature. This might explain at least parts of the diﬀerences
between the two model implementation.
Another explanation could be the way the model is solved. As it was shown previously,
when the Simulink model was validated and compared to Aspen, if the liquid is assumed
to be ideal there is relatively good agreement between the two models compared to if the
liquid is described by Wilson’s thermodynamic model. Since the interaction parameters
for the activity coeﬃcients are found in Aspen, it is likely during the solving of the models
Page 90 of 100
==PAGE:99==
12.4. Solving of the dynamic model
June, 2018
that the diﬀerences occurs.
The steady state rate based model is a nonlinear algebraic equation system which means
when solving iterative it is possible multiple solutions exist. With many equations that
need to be solved iterative there is a high possibility of multiple solutions. Especially if
Aspen Plus have additional model equations for the vapor and liquid ﬁlm.
In order to make the Matlab model more comparable with the Aspen model, it would be
a good idea to use the exact same conditions and parameters, e.g. reﬂux ratio, interfacial
area, total condenser etc., to the extent it is possible.
Furthermore, instead of going
from a single compartment to a complete tank model, the complete tank model could be
gradually build up by going from a single compartment model to two connected models
and thus gradually build the complete tank model. This way it might be possible to
identify where the deviation in the Matlab model from the Aspen model occurs, whether
this is in the inner tank, outer shell, reboiler, condenser, compressor or throttle valve.
12.4
Solving of the dynamic model
For the dynamic simulation of the complete tank model the computational time was long,
with a time-span of 400 seconds the simulations took approximately 5 hours. This is
due to the iterative solving of the algebraic equations which must be done for every time
step. One way to avoid the interfacial algebraic equations which are solved iterative is to
convert them to diﬀerential equations and that way avoid the iterative solving of algebraic
equations. However, this would require knowledge of the interfacial holdups which can be
diﬃcult to deﬁne and further increase the number of equations to account for the holdups.
Because of the long computational time it would be very time-consuming to design a
control conﬁguration and especially to tune the controllers.
For some of the dynamic cases the ethanol composition in the light product exceeded the
azeotrope. This is not physically possible, but it can be explained by the implementation
in Matlab. The Matlab model does not take into account the physical limitations of the
model, since no limits have been implemented. The reason for no limits being implemented
for e.g. the compositions is that it would make the model even slower. Furthermore, if a
suitable control conﬁguration is implemented the set points could be chosen so that the
limits are not violated.
For the investigations of the dynamic model response to disturbances in the feed it was
found that when the condenser holdup decrease the outputs become unstable and starts
drifting away from stable operation. It is therefore important, when designing a control
structure, to make sure the condenser holdup is controlled either implicit or explicit.
Page 91 of 100
==PAGE:100==
13. Conclusion
June, 2018
13
Conclusion
Both steady state and dynamic rate based distillation models were developed for a heat
integrated horizontal tank for the separation of the binary mixture of water and ethanol.
The model development was done in phases by starting with a single compartment model,
then make a multiple compartment adiabatic model and ﬁnally the multiple compartment
diabatic model. The multiple compartment models included an equilibrium stage for the
partial reboiler and condenser. Furthermore, the outer shell operates at a higher pressure
than the inner tank which is ensured by a compressor and a throttle valve between the
two. All the developed models were implemented and simulated in Matlab Simulink.
At ﬁrst the tank transfer rate coeﬃcients were found by assuming the interface between
the liquid and vapor phase was a ﬂat plane which also means there were no mixing of
the phases. Later it showed that this would not give suﬃcient interfacial area for the
mass transfer, so it was assumed the phases were mixed in such a way that the transfer
coeﬃcients could be found from a randomly packed column correlation.
Model validation was done for the Wilson activity coeﬃcient model and the transfer rate
model.
For the transfer rate model validation an existing Aspen model was used for
comparison and during this it was found that there was a signiﬁcant diﬀerence between
this and the Matlab Simulink model. Even after using a packed column correlation for
the transfer coeﬃcients there were still a diﬀerence, and therefore it was decided to focus
on making a dynamic heat integrated model in Matlab Simulink.
Based on the sensitivity analysis it was found it is paramount to keep the outer shell
interfacial area high to ensure a high ethanol content in the light product. Other important
factors are the mass and heat transfer coeﬃcients and the overall heat transfer coeﬃcient
between the inner tank and outer shell. These may, however, not be as easy to manipulate
as the interfacial area as this mostly depends on the mixing in the tank.
When the single compartment model was validated the tank model was made. First an
adiabatic tank model with the assumption of ﬂat plane interface (this means there were
no mixing of the phases) was made and when this proved not suitable the assumption
of packed column design (this means there were mixing of the phases) was made. This
assumption gave satisfying results of the products with regards to both compositions and
ﬂow rates.
After the adiabatic tank model was done heat integration was added between the outer
shell and inner tank and it was found approximately 550 kW of energy could be saved
on required energy for the condenser and reboiler without a signiﬁcant change in product
streams compared to the adiabatic model.
In steady state for the diabatic tank the heavy product, with a ﬂow rate of 95.059 mol/s,
Page 92 of 100
==PAGE:101==
14. Future works
June, 2018
has a ethanol composition of 1 mol% and the light product, with a ﬂow rate of 4.941
mol/s, has a ethanol composition of 81.2 mol%. These product values were achieved with
a condenser duty of 700 kW and a reboiler duty of 250 kW as well as a feed ﬂow of 100
mol/s with 5 mol% ethanol and 95 mol% water. With these duties the boilup and reﬂux
ratios were quite high, 1.21 and 55.31 respectively.
These reﬂux and boilup ratios for steady state operation are high and might not be a
good solution. Therefore, more work should be done to reduce these ratios and attempt
to match the results from the implemented model in Aspen in steady state. This could
for example be done by changing some of the properties and correlations to the exact
same as is used in the Aspen model.
Two cases were investigated for the dynamic model: +20% ethanol feed composition and
a 5% increase in the total feed ﬂow rate. From the results of these simulations it became
apparent that a control conﬁguration is necessary, especially for the condenser, in order
to keep the outputs from drifting.
Additionally a +5% change in the feed ethanol composition and a +1% change in feed
ﬂow rate were investigated to see the system response to smaller disturbances. The results
of these simulations once again proved the system was unstable, even for small changes
in initial feed conditions. This also supports the need of a control conﬁguration.
For these disturbances it was found that especially the condenser holdup had an eﬀect
on the stability of the system. It seemed that when the condenser holdup decreased the
dynamic model system became unstable.
Finally, a control design strategy was proposed based on the top-down bottom-up pro-
cedure for plantwide control. The actual implementation of a control conﬁguration was
not done, but the overall ideas of how to do this are given. It is believed that a control
structure, especially with regards to the molar holdup in the condenser and reﬂux stream
would make it possible to avoid the nodes from drifting.
14
Future works
The heat integrated horizontal tank is an interesting new type of distillation.
Some
suggestions for future projects on the diabatic horizontal distillation tank for fermentation
liquids are given below.
First the model implementation should be improved so the results from the Aspen model
can be reproduced with the Matlab model. For example, it can be tried to implement
the same property correlations and parameters used in the Aspen model in the Matlab
model, in the extent it is possible, and thereby making the two model implementations
Page 93 of 100
==PAGE:102==
14. Future works
June, 2018
more comparable. Furthermore, instead of deﬁning the heat duties for the reboiler and
condenser, the reﬂux and boilup ratios could be ﬁxed.
The condenser could also be
modeled as a total condenser instead of a partial condenser.
Then instead of going from a single compartment model to a complete tank model, the
complete tank model could be gradually built by connecting and testing more and more
compartments and ﬁnally the reboiler and condenser until the complete tank model is
made. This way it is possible to better get an idea of where the deviation between the
Matlab and the Aspen model occurs.
Another interesting project would be to actually design and implement a control conﬁgu-
ration for the horizontal tank, which is possible to do with the developed dynamic model.
The strategy presented in this thesis could serve as a starting point for this project.
The fermentation liquid simulated in this project was a binary mixture of ethanol and
water, however, many diﬀerent components are present in real fermentation liquids. It
is therefore recommended to expand the model to handle multiple component systems.
Fermentation liquids also contain solid residues and it should also be tried to include these
residues in the model.
Experimental work should be done to conﬁrm, improve or dismiss, the model developed
in this project. Furthermore, experiments to get better transfer rate correlations for this
type of set up should also be done. These experiments could be in laboratory, pilot plant
and industrial scale, dependent on the goal of the experiment.

relations are not necessary since the binary transfer coeﬃcients are the same for water
in ethanol and ethanol in water. The correlations are all found on the Thermal Fluid
Central website [34]. The densities for liquid (L) and vapor (V) water (W) are shown
below, in units of kg/m3.

A.2. Simulink implementations of multiple compartment model

A.2
Simulink implementations of multiple compartment model
The overall Simulink model of multiple compartments can be seen in Figure A.1.
The outer shell and inner tank boxes on the ﬁgure contain multiple models of the single
compartments as showed earlier and are connected so that the liquid entering compart-
ment j comes from compartment j-1 and the vapor comes from compartment j+1. This
can also be seen in Figure A.2.

H_Vjp12 = 38600 + 87.53*(T_Vjp1-352); %J/mol, enthalpy of ethanol vapor from prevoius stage
140
H_Ljm1 = H_Ljm11*xjm11 + H_Ljm12*xjm12; %J/mol enthalpy of liquid from previous stage
141
H_Vjp1 = H_Vjp11*yjp11 + H_Vjp12*yjp12; %J/mol enthalpy of vapor from previous stage
142
143
% Transfer cofficients
144
Tprop =y(5)-273.15; %C, correlations are for T in celsius
145
% Properties,
146
% Assume liquid is water and vapor phase is ethanol/air
147
% Densities
148
rho_wl =
exp(6.9094-2.0146*10^(-5)*Tprop - 5.9868*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 + 2.5921*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 - 9.3244*10^(-11)*Tprop^4 + ...
1.2103*10^(-13)*Tprop^5); %kg/m3, density of liquid water
149
rho_wv =
exp(-5.3225+6.8366*10^(-2)*Tprop - 2.7243*10^(-4)*Tprop^2 + 8.4522*10^(-7)*Tprop^3 - 1.65581*10^(-9)*Tprop^4 + ...
1.5514*10^(-12)*Tprop^5); %kg/m3, density of water vapor
150
151
mu_wl =
exp(9.7620-3.1154*10^(-2)*Tprop + 2.0029*10^(-4)*Tprop^2 - 9.5815*10^(-7)*Tprop^3 + 2.7772*10^(-9)*Tprop^4 - ...
3.5075*10^(-12)*Tprop^5)*10^(-7); %N s/m, viscosity of water liquid
152
mu_wv =
exp(4.3995+3.8789*10^(-3)*Tprop + 2.1181*10^(-5)*Tprop^2 - 3.4406*10^(-7)*Tprop^3 + 1.6730*10^(-9)*Tprop^4 - ...
2.8030*10^(-12)*Tprop^5)*10^(-7); %N s/m, viscosity of water vapor
153
154
lambda_wl =
exp(-5.6528*10^(-1) + 3.1743*10^(-3)*Tprop - 1.4392*10^(-5)*Tprop^2 - 1.3224*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + ...
2.5534*10^(-10)*Tprop^4 - 6.4454*10^(-13)*Tprop^5); %J/m s K, thermal conductivity of liquid water
155
lambda_wv =
exp(-4.0406 + 3.228*10^(-3)*Tprop + 5.3383*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 - 6.7139*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + 4.0967*10^(-10)*Tprop^4 - ...
6.9579*10^(-13)*Tprop^5); %J/m s K, thermal conductivity of water vapor
156
157
Cp_wl =
exp(1.4338 - 2.2638*10^(-4)*Tprop + 4.2819*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 - 2.7411*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + 1.4699*10^(-10)*Tprop^4 - ...
2.2589*10^(-13)*Tprop^5)*10^3; %J/kg K, specific heat capacity of liquid water
158
Cp_wv =
exp(6.2084*10^(-1) + 3.1420*10^(-4)*Tprop + 1.6110*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 + 4.0156*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + 3.4841*10^(-11)*Tprop^4 - ...
2.0709*10^(-13)*Tprop^5)*10^3; %J/kg K, specific heat capacity of water vapor
159
160
D_wel = 0.69*10^(-9); %m2/s, diffusion coefficient of water in ethanol liquid.
161
D_wav = 24*10^(-6); %m2/s, diffusion coefficient of water in air (ethanol)
162
163
% Dimensionless numbers
164
Re_wv =
rho_wv*u_v*l_0/mu_wv; %Reynolds number for vapor water phase
VII
==PAGE:116==
A.3. Matlab scripts and s-functions
June, 2018
165
Re_wl =
rho_wl*u_l*l_0/mu_wl; %Reynolds number for liquid water phase
166
Sc_wv =
mu_wv/(rho_wv*D_wav); %Schmidt number for vapor water phase, water in air
167
Sc_wl =
mu_wl/(rho_wl*D_wel); %Schmidt number for liquid water phase, water in water
168
Pr_wv =
Cp_wv*mu_wv/lambda_wv; %Prandtl number for vapor phase water
169
Pr_wl =
Cp_wl*mu_wl/lambda_wl; %Prandtl number for liquid phase water
170
Le_wv = Sc_wv/Pr_wv; % Lewis number for vapor phase water
171
Le_wl = Sc_wl/Pr_wl; % Lewis number for liquid phase water
172
173
% From Transport Phenomena: Flat plate geometry
174
%jD_wv =
0.332*Re_wv^(-1/2); %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for diffusion, water vapor
175
%jD_wl =
0.332*Re_wl^(-1/2); %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for diffusion, water liquid
176
%jH_wv =
jD_wv; %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for heat, water vapor
177
%jH_wl =
jD_wl; %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for heat, water liquid
178
179
% Mass and heat transfer coefficient
180
% jD = Sh/(Re Sc^(1/3)), jH = Nu/(Re Pr^(1/3))
181
% Nu = h*l0/lambda, Sh = k*l0/D_AB
182
% flat plane
183
%k_wev =
jD_wv*Re_wv*Sc_wv^(1/3)*D_wav*1/l_0; %m/s, mass transfer coefficient for water in ethanol(air) in vapor phase
184
%k_wel =
jD_wl*Re_wl*Sc_wl^(1/3)*D_wel*1/l_0; %m/s, mass transfer coefficient for water in ethanol liquid phase
185
186
% From Multi component mass transfer. Randomly packed column, Bravo & Fair.
187
k_wev = 50*D_wel*2*Re_wv^(0.7)*Sc_wv^(0.333)*(50*l_0)^(-2); % m/s, ap = 50 m2/m3, dp = 0.005 m, A = 2 for dp<0.012. dp = l_0
188
k_wel = 0.0051*Re_wl^(0.667)*Sc_wl^(-0.5)*(50*l_0)^(0.4)*1/(rho_wl/(mu_wl*9.81))^0.333; %m/s
189
190
% Assigning the mass transfer coefficients in a matrix
191
k_v(1,1) =
0;
192
k_v(1,2) =
k_wev;
193
k_v(2,1) =
k_v(1,2);
194
k_v(2,2) =
0;
195
k_l(1,1) =
0;
196
k_l(1,2) =
k_wel;
197
k_l(2,1) =
k_l(1,2);
198
k_l(2,2) =
0;
199
% Flat plane
200
%h_wv =
jH_wv*Re_wv*Pr_wv^(1/3)*lambda_wv*1/l_0; %J/m2 s K, heat transfer coefficient for water in vapor phase
201
%h_wl =
jH_wl*Re_wl*Pr_wl^(1/3)*lambda_wl*1/l_0; %J/m2 s K, heat transfer coefficient for water in liquid phase
202
203
% Randomly packed column, Chilton-Colburn, sep book
204
h_wv = k_wev*rho_wv*Cp_wv*Le_wv^(2/3); % J/m2 s K
205
h_wl = k_wel*rho_wl*Cp_wl*Le_wl^(1/2); % J/m2 s K
206
207
h_v =
h_wv; % W/m2 K, total heat transfer coefficient in vapor phase
208
h_l =
h_wl; % W/m2 K, total heat transfer coefficient in liquid phase
209
210
% Activity coefficient for binary system
211
% Lambda matrix
212
Lambda(1,1) = 1;
213
Lambda(2,2) = 1;
214
Lambda(1,2) = exp(-0.0503 - 69.6372./y(5)); % From Aspen documentation
215
Lambda(2,1) = exp(-2.5035 + 346.151./y(5)); % From Aspen documentation
216
% S
217
S1 =
y(12)+y(13)*Lambda(1,2);
218
S2 =
y(13)+y(12)*Lambda(2,1);
219
% Q
220
% First composition derivatives
221
Q1 =
-log(S1) - y(12)/S1 - y(13) * Lambda(2,1)/S2;
222
Q2 =
-log(S2) - y(13)/S2 - y(12) * Lambda(1,2)/S1;
223
% Second composition derivatives
224
Q11 =
-2/S1 + y(12)/S1^2 + y(13)*Lambda(2,1)^2/S2^2;
225
Q12 =
-Lambda(1,2)/S1 - Lambda(2,1)/S2 + y(12)*Lambda(1,2)/S1^2 + y(13)*Lambda(2,1)/S2^2;
226
Q21 =
Q12;
227
Q22 =
-2/S2 + y(13)/S2^2 + y(12)*Lambda(1,2)^2/S1^2;
228
% Activity coefficients
229
lngamma1 =
y(12)+y(13)+Q1;
230
lngamma2 =
y(12)+y(13)+Q2;
231
gammaWilson1 =
exp(lngamma1);
232
gammaWilson2 =
exp(lngamma2);
233
% Thermodynamic factor
234
Gamma(1,1) =
Q11 - y(12)*Q11 - y(13)*Q21;
235
Gamma(1,2) =
Q12 - y(12)*Q12 - y(13)*Q22;
236
Gamma(2,1) =
Q21 - y(13)*Q21 - y(12)*Q11;
237
Gamma(2,2) =
Q22 - y(13)*Q22 - y(12)*Q12;
238
239
% Vapor pressure from Antoine
240
% For water, the Antoine parameters are
241
% From Aspen
242
Psatw = exp(73.649 - 7258./(y(5))-7.3037.*log(y(5)) + 4.1653e-6 .* y(5).^2); % Pa, from Aspen, extended Antoine
243
Psate = exp(73.304 - 7122.3./(y(5)) -7.1424.*log(y(5)) + 2.8853e-6 .*y(5).^2); %Pa, from Aspen
244
245
% Equilibrium ratio
246
Keq1 =
Psatw*gammaWilson1/P;
247
Keq2 =
Psate*gammaWilson2/P;
248
249
% Transfer rates, 2 component system only kappa(1,1) is necessary. Sum of flux = 0
250
RV(1,1) =
y(14)/k_v(1,2) + y(15)/k_v(1,2); %inverse transfer coefficient, vapor
251
RL(1,1) =
y(12)/k_l(1,2) + y(13)/k_l(1,2); %inverse transfer coefficient, liquid
252
253
kappa_V(1,1) =
RV(1,1)^(-1); %corrected transfer coefficient, vapor
254
kappa_L(1,1) =
RL(1,1)^(-1)*Gamma(1,1); %corrected transfer coefficient, liquid
255
256
J_V1 =
c_TV*kappa_V(1,1)*(y(10)-y(14)); %mol/s m2, water flux in vapor phase
VIII
==PAGE:117==
A.3. Matlab scripts and s-functions
June, 2018
257
J_V2 =
-J_V1; %mol/s m2, ethanol flux in vapor phase
258
J_L1 =
c_TL*kappa_L(1,1)*(y(12)-y(8)); %mol/s m2, water flux in liquid phase
259
J_L2 =
-J_L1; %mol/s m2, ethanol flux in liquid phase
260
261
N_T =
y(6)+y(7); %mol/s, total material transfer rate
262
N_V1 =
aI*J_V1 + y(10)*N_T;
%mol/s, transfer rate of water vapor
263
N_V2 =
aI*J_V2 + y(11)*N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of ethanol vapor
264
N_L1 =
aI*J_L1 + y(8)*N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of water liquid
265
N_L2 =
aI*J_L2 + y(9)*N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of ethanol liquid
266
267
e_L =
aI*h_l*(y(5)-y(3)) + (N_L1*H_Lpar1+N_L2*H_Lpar2); %J/s, heat transfer rate, liquid
268
e_V =
aI*h_v*(y(4)-y(5)) + (N_V1*H_Vpar1+N_V2*H_Vpar2); %J/s, heat transfer rate, vapor
269

%% Model equations
271
% Variables y:
272
% y = [x1, x2, x3,
x4,

x9,

273
% y = [Lj, Vj, TLj, TVj, TIj, N1j, N2j, x1j, x2j, y1j, y2j, xI1j, xI2j, yI1j, yI2j]
274
% Functions F
275
F(1) = (1+r_L)*y(1) - Ljm1 - F_L - (y(6)+y(7));
% Total liquid material balance
276
F(2) = (1+r_V)*y(2) - Vjp1 - F_V + (y(6)+y(7));
% Total vapor material balance
277
F(3) = (1+r_L)*y(1)*y(8) - Ljm1*xjm1(1) - f_L(1) - N_L1;
% Liquid water balance
278
F(4) = (1+r_L)*y(1)*y(9) - Ljm1*xjm1(2) - f_L(2) - N_L2;
% Liquid ethanol balance
279
F(5) = (1+r_V)*y(2)*y(10) - Vjp1*yjp1(1) - f_V(1) + N_V1;
% Vapor water balance
280
F(6) = (1+r_V)*y(2)*y(11) - Vjp1*yjp1(2) - f_V(2) + N_V2;
% Vapor ethanol balance
281
F(7) = (1+r_L)*y(1)*H_L - Ljm1*H_Ljm1 - F_L*H_LF + Q_L - e_L;
% Liquid phase energy balance
282
F(8) = (1+r_L)*y(2)*H_V - Vjp1*H_Vjp1 - F_V*H_VF + Q_V + e_V;
% Vapor phase energy balance
283
F(9) = e_V - e_L;
% Interfacial energy balance
284
F(10) = y(6) - N_L1;
% Rate equation for water in liquid phase
285
F(11) = y(6) - N_V1;
% Rate equation for water in vapor phase
286
F(12) = (y(12)+y(13)) - 1;
% Summation equation for liquid interface
287
F(13) = (y(14)+y(15)) - 1;
% Summation equation for vapor interface
288
F(14) = Keq1 * y(12) - y(14);
% Equilibrium equation for water
289
F(15) = Keq2 * y(13) - y(15);
% Equilibrium equation for ethanol
290
sys = F;

156
mu_wv =
exp(4.3995 + 3.8789*10^(-3)*Tprop + 2.1181*10^(-5)*Tprop^2 - 3.4406*10^(-7)*Tprop^3 + 1.6730*10^(-9)*Tprop^4 - ...
2.8030*10^(-12)*Tprop^5)*10^(-7); %N s/m, viscosity of water vapor
157
158
lambda_wl =
exp(-5.6528*10^(-1) + 3.1743*10^(-3)*Tprop - 1.4392*10^(-5)*Tprop^2 - 1.3224*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + ...
2.5534*10^(-10)*Tprop^4 - 6.4454*10^(-13)*Tprop^5); %J/m s K, thermal conductivity of liquid water
159
lambda_wv =
exp(-4.0406 + 3.228*10^(-3)*Tprop + 5.3383*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 - 6.7139*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + 4.0967*10^(-10)*Tprop^4 - ...
6.9579*10^(-13)*Tprop^5); %J/m s K, thermal conductivity of water vapor
160
161
Cp_wl =
exp(1.4338-2.2638*10^(-4)*Tprop + 4.2819*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 - 2.7411*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + 1.4699*10^(-10)*Tprop^4 - ...
2.2589*10^(-13)*Tprop^5)*10^3; %J/kg K, specific heat capacity of liquid water
162
Cp_wv =
exp(6.2084*10^(-1) + 3.1420*10^(-4)*Tprop + 1.6110*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 + 4.0156*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + 3.4841*10^(-11)*Tprop^4 - ...
2.0709*10^(-13)*Tprop^5)*10^3; %J/kg K, specific heat capacity of water vapor
163
164
D_wel = 0.69*10^(-9); %m/2, diffusion coefficient of water in ethanol liquid
165
D_wav = 24*10^(-6); %m2/s, diffusion coefficient of water in air (ethanol)
166
167
% Dimensionless numbers
168
Re_wv =
rho_wv*u_v*l_0/mu_wv; %Reynolds number for vapor water phase
169
Re_wl =
rho_wl*u_l*l_0/mu_wl; %Reynolds number for liquid water phase
170
Sc_wv =
mu_wv/(rho_wv*D_wav); %Schmidt number for vapor water phase, water in air
171
Sc_wl =
mu_wl/(rho_wl*D_wel); %Schmidt number for liquid water phase, water in water
172
Pr_wv =
Cp_wv*mu_wv/lambda_wv; %Prandtl number for vapor phase water
173
Pr_wl =
Cp_wl*mu_wl/lambda_wl; %Prandtl number for liquid phase water
174
Le_wv = Sc_wv/Pr_wv; % Lewis number for vapor phase water
175
Le_wl = Sc_wl/Pr_wl; % Lewis number for liquid phase water
176
177
% From Transport Phenomena: Flat plate geometry
178
%jD_wv =
0.332*Re_wv^(-1/2); %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for diffusion, water vapor
179
%jD_wl =
0.332*Re_wl^(-1/2); %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for diffusion, water liquid
180
%jH_wv =
jD_wv; %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for heat, water vapor
181
%jH_wl =
jD_wl; %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for heat, water liquid
182
183
% Mass and heat transfer coefficient
184
% jD = Sh/(Re Sc^(1/3)), jH = Nu/(Re Pr^(1/3))
185
% Nu = h*l0/lambda, Sh = k*l0/D_AB
186
% flat plane
187
%k_wev =
jD_wv*Re_wv*Sc_wv^(1/3)*D_wav*1/l_0; %m/s, mass transfer coefficient for water in ethanol(air) in vapor phase
188
%k_wel =
jD_wl*Re_wl*Sc_wl^(1/3)*D_wel*1/l_0; %m/s, mass transfer coefficient for water in ethanol liquid phase
189
190
% From Multi component mass transfer. Randomly packed column, Bravo & Fair.
191
k_wev = 50*D_wel*2*Re_wv^(0.7)*Sc_wv^(0.333)*(50*l_0)^(-2); % m/s, ap = 50 m2/m3, dp = 0.005 m, A = 2 for dp<0.012. dp = l_0
192
k_wel = 0.0051*Re_wl^(0.667)*Sc_wl^(-0.5)*(50*l_0)^(0.4)*1/(rho_wl/(mu_wl*9.81))^0.333; %m/s
193
194
% Assigning the mass transfer coefficients in a matrix
195
k_v(1,1) =
0;
196
k_v(1,2) =
k_wev;
197
k_v(2,1) =
k_v(1,2);
198
k_v(2,2) =
0;
199
k_l(1,1) =
0;
200
k_l(1,2) =
k_wel;
201
k_l(2,1) =
k_l(1,2);
202
k_l(2,2) =
0;
203
% Flat plane
204
%h_wv =
jH_wv*Re_wv*Pr_wv^(1/3)*lambda_wv*1/l_0; %J/m2 s K, heat transfer coefficient for water in vapor phase
205
%h_wl =
jH_wl*Re_wl*Pr_wl^(1/3)*lambda_wl*1/l_0; %J/m2 s K, heat transfer coefficient for water in liquid phase
206
207
% Randomly packed column, Chilton-Colburn, sep book
208
h_wv = k_wev*rho_wv*Cp_wv*Le_wv^(2/3); % J/m2 s K
209
h_wl = k_wel*rho_wl*Cp_wl*Le_wl^(1/2); % J/m2 s K
210
211
h_v =
h_wv; % W/m2 K, total heat transfer coefficient in vapor phase
212
h_l =
h_wl; % W/m2 K, total heat transfer coefficient in liquid phase
213
214
% Activity coefficient for binary system
215
% Lambda matrix
216
Lambda(1,1) = 1;
217
Lambda(2,2) = 1;
218
Lambda(1,2) = exp(-0.0503 - 69.6372./y(5)); % From Aspen documentation
219
Lambda(2,1) = exp(-2.5035 + 346.151./y(5)); % From Aspen documentation
220
%S
221
S1 =
y(12)+y(13)*Lambda(1,2);
222
S2 =
y(13)+y(12)*Lambda(2,1);
223
%Q
XI
==PAGE:120==
A.3. Matlab scripts and s-functions
June, 2018
224
%First composition derivatives
225
Q1 =
-log(S1) - y(12)/S1 - y(13) * Lambda(2,1)/S2;
226
Q2 =
-log(S2) - y(13)/S2 - y(12) * Lambda(1,2)/S1;
227
%Second composition derivatives
228
Q11 =
-2/S1 + y(12)/S1^2 + y(13)*Lambda(2,1)^2/S2^2;
229
Q12 =
-Lambda(1,2)/S1 - Lambda(2,1)/S2 + y(12)*Lambda(1,2)/S1^2 + y(13)*Lambda(2,1)/S2^2;
230
Q21 =
Q12;
231
Q22 =
-2/S2 + y(13)/S2^2 + y(12)*Lambda(1,2)^2/S1^2;
232
%Activity coefficients
233
lngamma1 =
y(12)+y(13)+Q1;
234
lngamma2 =
y(12)+y(13)+Q2;
235
gammaWilson1 =
exp(lngamma1);
236
gammaWilson2 =
exp(lngamma2);
237
% Thermodynamic factor
238
Gamma(1,1) =
Q11 - y(12)*Q11 - y(13)*Q21;
239
Gamma(1,2) =
Q12 - y(12)*Q12 - y(13)*Q22;
240
Gamma(2,1) =
Q21 - y(13)*Q21 - y(12)*Q11;
241
Gamma(2,2) =
Q22 - y(13)*Q22 - y(12)*Q12;
242
243
% Vapor pressure from Antoine
244
%For water, the Antoine parameters are
245
%From Aspen
246
Psatw = exp(73.649 - 7258./(y(5))-7.3037.*log(y(5)) + 4.1653e-6 .* y(5).^2); % Pa, from Aspen, extended Antoine
247
Psate = exp(73.304 - 7122.3./(y(5)) -7.1424.*log(y(5)) + 2.8853e-6 .*y(5).^2); %Pa, from Aspen
248
249
%Equilibrium ratio
250
Keq1 =
Psatw*gammaWilson1/P;
251
Keq2 =
Psate*gammaWilson2/P;
252
253
% Transfer rates, 2 component system only kappa (1,1) is necessary. Sum of flux = 0
254
RV(1,1) =
y(14)/k_v(1,2) + y(15)/k_v(1,2); % inverse transfer coefficients, vapor
255
RL(1,1) =
y(12)/k_l(1,2) + y(13)/k_l(1,2); % inverse transfer coefficients, liquid
256
257
kappa_V(1,1) =
RV(1,1)^(-1); % corrected vapor transfer coefficient
258
kappa_L(1,1) =
RL(1,1)^(-1)*Gamma(1,1); % corrected liquid transfer coefficient
259
260
J_V1 =
c_TV*kappa_V(1,1)*(y(10)-y(14)); %mol/s m2, water flux in vapor phase
261
J_V2 =
-J_V1; %mol/s m2, ethanol flux in vapor phase
262
J_L1 =
c_TL*kappa_L(1,1)*(y(12)-y(8)); %mol/s m2, water flux in liquid phase
263
J_L2 =
-J_L1; %mol/s m2, ethanol flux in liquid phase
264
265
N_T =
y(6)+y(7); %mol/s, total material transfer rate
266
N_V1 =
aI*J_V1 + y(10) *N_T;
%mol/s, transfer rate of water vapor
267
N_V2 =
aI*J_V2 + y(11) *N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of ethanol vapor
268
N_L1 =
aI*J_L1 + y(8) *N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of water liquid
269
N_L2 =
aI*J_L2 + y(9) *N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of ethanol liquid
270
271
e_L =
aI*h_l*(y(5)-y(3)) + (N_L1*H_Lpar1+N_L2*H_Lpar2); %J/s, heat transfer rate, liquid
272
e_V =
aI*h_v*(y(4)-y(5)) + (N_V1*H_Vpar1+N_V2*H_Vpar2); %J/s, heat transfer rate, vapor
273
%% Equations
274
%Variables y:
275
%y = [x1, x2, x3,
x4,
x5,
x6,
x7,
x8,
x9,
x10, x11, x12,
x13,
x14,
x15,
x16,
x17,
x18, x19, x20,
x21,
x22,
x23]
276
%y = [Lj, Vj, TLj, TVj, TIj, N1j, N2j, x1j, x2j, y1j, y2j, xI1j, xI2j, yI1j, yI2j, MTjL, MTjV, EjL, EjV, MjL1, MjL2, MjV1, MjV2]
277
%Functions F
278
F(1) = (1+r_L)*y(1) - Ljm1 - F_L - (y(6)+y(7));
% Total liquid material balance
279
F(2) = (1+r_V)*y(2) - Vjp1 - F_V + (y(6)+y(7));
% Total vapor material balance
280
F(3) = (1+r_L)*y(1)*y(8) - Ljm1*xjm1(1) - f_L(1) - N_L1;
% Liquid water balance
281
F(4) = (1+r_L)*y(1)*y(9) - Ljm1*xjm1(2) - f_L(2) - N_L2;
% Liquid ethanol balance
282
F(5) = (1+r_V)*y(2)*y(10) - Vjp1*yjp1(1) - f_V(1) + N_V1;
% Vapor water balance
283
F(6) = (1+r_V)*y(2)*y(11) - Vjp1*yjp1(2) - f_V(2) + N_V2;
% Vapor ethanol balance
284
F(7) = (1+r_L)*y(1)*H_L - Ljm1*H_Ljm1 - F_L*H_LF + Q_L - e_L;
% Liquid phase energy balance
285
F(8) = (1+r_L)*y(2)*H_V - Vjp1*H_Vjp1 - F_V*H_VF + Q_V + e_V;
% Vapor phase energy balance
286
F(9) = e_V - e_L;
% Interfacial energy balance
287
F(10) = y(6) - N_L1;
% Rate equation for water in liquid phase
288
F(11) = y(7) - N_V2;
% Rate equation for water in vapor phase
289
F(12) = (y(12) + y(13))-1;
% Summation equation for liquid interface
290
F(13) = (y(14) + y(15))-1;
% Summation equation for vapor interface
291
F(14) = Keq1 * y(12) - y(14);
% Equilibrium equation for water
292
F(15) = Keq2 * y(13) - y(15);
% Equilibrium equation for ethanol
293
F(16) = y(16) - Phi_L * Vol * c_TL;
% Liquid holdup balance
294
F(17) = y(17) - (1-Phi_L)*Vol * c_TV;
% Vapor holdup balance
295
F(18) = y(8) - y(20)/y(16);
% Liquid water holdup balance
296
F(19) = y(9) - y(21)/y(16);
% Liquid ethanol holdup balance
297
F(20) = y(10) - y(22)/y(17);
% Vapor water holdup balance
298
F(21) = y(11) - y(23)/y(17);
% Vapor ethanol holdup balance
299
F(22) = y(18) - y(16)*H_L;
% Energy liquid holdup balance
300
F(23) = y(19) - y(17)*H_V;
% Energy vapor holdup balance
301
sys = F;
System function used for dynamic single compartment simulation.
dyn_SC_DAE_sfun.m

% mdlDerivatives

function sys=mdlDerivatives(t,x,u)
42
% Sorting inputs
43
y = u(1:7);
% Interface variables, x is the time dependent values
44
par = u(8:21); % parameters
45
feed = u(22:31); % feed, (also heat duties and removed flow fractions)
46
preL = u(32:35); % liquid from previous stage
47
preV = u(36:39); % vapor from previous stage
48
% x = TjL, TjV, x1j, x2j, y1j, y2j, MTjL, MTjV, EjL, EjV, M1jL, M2jL, M1jV, M2jV, Lj,
Vj
49
% x = y1,
y2,
y3,
y4,
y5,
y6,

y9,

y12,
y13,
y14,
y15, y16
50
% y = TjI, N1j, N2j, x1jI, x2jI, y1jI and y2jI
51
% y = y17, y18, y19, y20,
y21,
y22
and y23
52
%%%% Binary system Water (1) - Ethanol (2)
53
%par = [R N P L0 D u_v u_l Phi_L h l_0 aI aC aC_V aC_L]
54
R = par(1); %J/K mol, gas constant not used
55

56

57
% Dimensions
58

59

60

63

66

67

68

69

Vol = aC*L0/N; %m3, stage volume
73

89
Q_V = feed(8); %J/s, removed heat from vapor.
90
%Removed from stream

r_L = feed(9); %fraction of removed liquid after stage
92
r_V = feed(10); %fraction of removed vapor after stage
93
%From previous stage
94
%preL = [Ljm1, T_Ljm1, xjm11, xjm12]; % Previous liquid
95
%preV = [Vjp1, T_Vjp1, yjp11, yjp12]; % Previous vapor

A.3. Matlab scripts and s-functions
June, 2018
96
Vjp1 = preV(1); %mol/s, molar flow of vapor from previous stage
97
Ljm1 = preL(1); %mol/s, molar flow of liquid from previus stage
98

99

%% Properties
106

107
c_TV = (x(16))/(aC_V*u_v);
%mol/m3, vapor concentration
108
c_TL = (x(15))/(aC_L*u_l); %mol/m3, liquid concentration
109
% Enthalpies

% Constant heat capacities
111
CP_wL = 75; %J/K mol, heat capacity for liquid water
112
CP_eL = 112.4; %J/K mol, heat capacity for liquid ethanol
113
CP_wV = 27; %J/K mol, heat capacity for vapor water
114
CP_eV = 87.53; %J/K mol, heat capacity for vapor ethanol

%Feed
116
H_wLF =
40660 + CP_wL*(T_LF-373.15); %J/mol, enthalpy of water liquid feed
117
H_eLF = 38600 + CP_eL*(T_LF-352); %J/mol, enthalpy of ethanol liquid feed
118
H_wVF = 40660 + CP_wV*(T_VF-373.15); %J/mol enthalpy of water vapor feed
119
H_eVF = 38600 + CP_eV*(T_VF-352); %J/mol, enthalpy of ethanol vapor feed
120
if F_L == 0 % If statement to avoid division by zero.
121
H_LF = 0;
122
else
123
H_LF = H_wLF*(f_L1/F_L) + H_eLF*(f_L2/F_L); %J/mol, total liquid feed enthalpy
124
end
125
if F_V == 0 % If statement to avoid division by 0.
126
H_VF = 0;
127
else
128
H_VF = H_wVF*(f_V1/F_V) + H_eVF*(f_V2/F_V); %J/mol, total vapor feed enthalpy
129
end
130
%Phase
131
H_wL =
40660 + CP_wL*(x(1)-373.15); %J/mol, enthalpy of water liquid
132
H_eL =
38600 + CP_eL*(x(1)-352); %J/mol, enthalpy of ethanol liquid
133
H_wV =
40660 + CP_wV*(x(2)-373.15); %J/mol enthalpy of water vapor feed,
134
H_eV =
38600 + CP_eV*(x(2)-352); %J/mol, enthalpy of ethanol vapor
135
136
H_L =
H_wL*x(3) + H_eL*x(4); %J/mol, total liquid enthalpy
137
H_Lpar1 =
H_wL; %J/mol, enthalpy of liquid water
138
H_Lpar2 =
H_eL; %J/mol, enthalpy of liquid ethanol
139
H_V =
H_wV*x(5) + H_eV*x(6); %J/mol, total vapor enthalpy
140
H_Vpar1 =
H_wV; %J/mol, enthalpy of vapor water
141
H_Vpar2 =
H_eV; %J/mol, enthalpy of vapor ethanol
142
143
H_Ljm11 =
40660 + CP_wL*(T_Ljm1-373.15); %J/mol, enthalpy of water liquid from previous stage
144
H_Ljm12 = 38600 + CP_eL*(T_Ljm1-352); %J/mol, enthalpy of ethanol liquid from prevoius stage

H_Vjp12 = 38600 + CP_eV*(T_Vjp1-352); %J/mol, enthalpy of ethanol vapor from prevoius stage

% Transfer cofficients
150
Tprop =
y(1)-273.15; %C, correlations are for T in celsius
151
%Properties,
152
%Assume liquid is water and vapor phase is ethanol/air
153
%Densities
154
rho_wl =
exp(6.9094 - 2.0146*10^(-5)*Tprop - 5.9868*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 + 2.5921*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 - 9.3244*10^(-11)*Tprop^4 + ...
1.2103*10^(-13)*Tprop^5); %kg/m3, density of liquid water
155
rho_wv =
exp(-5.3225 + 6.8366*10^(-2)*Tprop - 2.7243*10^(-4)*Tprop^2 + 8.4522*10^(-7)*Tprop^3 - 1.65581*10^(-9)*Tprop^4 + ...
1.5514*10^(-12)*Tprop^5); %kg/m3, density of water vapor
156
157
mu_wl =
exp(9.7620 - 3.1154*10^(-2)*Tprop + 2.0029*10^(-4)*Tprop^2 - 9.5815*10^(-7)*Tprop^3 + 2.7772*10^(-9)*Tprop^4 - ...
3.5075*10^(-12)*Tprop^5)*10^(-7); %N s/m, viscosity of water liquid
158
mu_wv =
exp(4.3995 + 3.8789*10^(-3)*Tprop + 2.1181*10^(-5)*Tprop^2 - 3.4406*10^(-7)*Tprop^3 + 1.6730*10^(-9)*Tprop^4 - ...
2.8030*10^(-12)*Tprop^5)*10^(-7); %N s/m, viscosity of water vapor
159
160
lambda_wl =
exp(-5.6528*10^(-1) + 3.1743*10^(-3)*Tprop - 1.4392*10^(-5)*Tprop^2 - 1.3224*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + ...
2.5534*10^(-10)*Tprop^4 - 6.4454*10^(-13)*Tprop^5); %J/m s K, thermal conductivity of liquid water
161
lambda_wv =
exp(-4.0406 + 3.228*10^(-3)*Tprop + 5.3383*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 - 6.7139*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + 4.0967*10^(-10)*Tprop^4 - ...
6.9579*10^(-13)*Tprop^5); %J/m s K, thermal conductivity of water vapor
162
163
Cp_wl =
exp(1.4338 - 2.2638*10^(-4)*Tprop + 4.2819*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 - 2.7411*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + 1.4699*10^(-10)*Tprop^4 - ...
2.2589*10^(-13)*Tprop^5)*10^3; %J/kg K, specific heat capacity of liquid water
164
Cp_wv =
exp(6.2084*10^(-1) + 3.1420*10^(-4)*Tprop + 1.6110*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 + 4.0156*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 + 3.4841*10^(-11)*Tprop^4 - ...
2.0709*10^(-13)*Tprop^5)*10^3; %J/kg K, specific heat capacity of water vapor
165
166
D_wel = 0.69*10^(-9); %m/2, diffusion coefficient of water in ethanol liquid.
167
D_wav = 24*10^(-6); %m2/s, diffusion coefficient of water in air (ethanol)
168
169
%Dimensionless numbers
170
Re_wv =
rho_wv*u_v*l_0/mu_wv; %Reynolds number for vapor water phase
171
Re_wl =
rho_wl*u_l*l_0/mu_wl; %Reynolds number for liquid water phase
172
Sc_wv =
mu_wv/(rho_wv*D_wav); %Schmidt number for vapor water phase, water in air
173
Sc_wl =
mu_wl/(rho_wl*D_wel); %Schmidt number for liquid water phase, water in water
174
Pr_wv =
Cp_wv*mu_wv/lambda_wv; %Prandtl number for vapor phase water
175
Pr_wl =
Cp_wl*mu_wl/lambda_wl; %Prandtl number for liquid phase water
176
Le_wv = Sc_wv/Pr_wv; % Lewis number for vapor phase water
177
Le_wl = Sc_wl/Pr_wl; % Lewis number for liquid phase water
178
179
% From Transport Phenomena: Flat plate geometry
XIV
==PAGE:123==
A.3. Matlab scripts and s-functions
June, 2018
180
%jD_wv =
0.332*Re_wv^(-1/2); %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for diffusion, water vapor
181
%jD_wl =
0.332*Re_wl^(-1/2); %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for diffusion, water liquid
182
%jH_wv =
jD_wv; %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for heat, water vapor
183
%jH_wl =
jD_wl; %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for heat, water liquid
184
185
% Mass and heat transfer coefficient
186
% jD = Sh/(Re Sc^(1/3)), jH = Nu/(Re Pr^(1/3))
187
% Nu = h*l0/lambda, Sh = k*l0/D_AB
188
% flat plane
189
%k_wev =
jD_wv*Re_wv*Sc_wv^(1/3)*D_wav*1/l_0; %m/s, mass transfer coefficient for water in ethanol(air) in vapor phase
190
%k_wel =
jD_wl*Re_wl*Sc_wl^(1/3)*D_wel*1/l_0; %m/s, mass transfer coefficient for water in ethanol liquid phase
191
192
% From Multi component mass transfer. Randomly packed column, Bravo & Fair.
193
k_wev = 50*D_wel*2*Re_wv^(0.7)*Sc_wv^(0.333)*(50*l_0)^(-2); % m/s, ap = 50 m2/m3, dp = 0.005 m, A = 2 for dp<0.012. dp = l_0
194
k_wel = 0.0051*Re_wl^(0.667)*Sc_wl^(-0.5)*(50*l_0)^(0.4)*1/(rho_wl/(mu_wl*9.81))^0.333; %m/s
195
196
% Assigning the mass transfer coefficients in a matrix
197
k_v(1,1) =
0;
198
k_v(1,2) =
k_wev;
199
k_v(2,1) =
k_v(1,2);
200
k_v(2,2) =
0;
201
k_l(1,1) =
0;
202
k_l(1,2) =
k_wel;
203
k_l(2,1) =
k_l(1,2);
204
k_l(2,2) =
0;
205
% Flat plane
206
%h_wv =
jH_wv*Re_wv*Pr_wv^(1/3)*lambda_wv*1/l_0; %J/m2 s K, heat transfer coefficient for water in vapor phase
207
%h_wl =
jH_wl*Re_wl*Pr_wl^(1/3)*lambda_wl*1/l_0; %J/m2 s K, heat transfer coefficient for water in liquid phase
208
209
% Randomly packed column, Chilton-Colburn, sep book
210
h_wv = k_wev*rho_wv*Cp_wv*Le_wv^(2/3); % J/m2 s K
211
h_wl = k_wel*rho_wl*Cp_wl*Le_wl^(1/2); % J/m2 s K
212
213
h_v =
h_wv; % W/m2 K, total heat transfer coefficient in vapor phase
214
h_l =
h_wl; % W/m2 K, total heat transfer coefficient in liquid phase
215
216
% Activity coefficient for binary system
217
% Lambda matrix
218
Lambda(1,1) = 1;
219
Lambda(2,2) = 1;
220
Lambda(1,2) = exp(-0.0503 - 69.6372./y(1)); % From Aspen documentation
221
Lambda(2,1) = exp(-2.5035 + 346.151./y(1)); % From Aspen documentation
222
%S
223
S1 =
y(4)+y(5)*Lambda(1,2);
224
S2 =
y(5)+y(4)*Lambda(2,1);
225
%Q
226
%First composition derivatives
227
Q1 =
-log(S1) - y(4)/S1 - y(5) * Lambda(2,1)/S2;
228
Q2 =
-log(S2) - y(5)/S2 - y(4) * Lambda(1,2)/S1;
229
%Second composition derivatives
230
Q11 =
-2/S1 + y(4)/S1^2 + y(5)*Lambda(2,1)^2/S2^2;
231
Q12 =
-Lambda(1,2)/S1 - Lambda(2,1)/S2 + y(4)*Lambda(1,2)/S1^2 + y(5)*Lambda(2,1)/S2^2;
232
Q21 =
Q12;
233
Q22 =
-2/S2 + y(5)/S2^2 + y(4)*Lambda(1,2)^2/S1^2;
234
%Activity coefficients
235
lngamma1 =
y(4)+y(5)+Q1;
236
lngamma2 =
y(4)+y(5)+Q2;
237
gammaWilson1 =
exp(lngamma1);
238
gammaWilson2 =
exp(lngamma2);
239
% Thermodynamic factor
240
Gamma(1,1) =
Q11 - y(4)*Q11 - y(5)*Q21;
241
Gamma(1,2) =
Q12 - y(4)*Q12 - y(5)*Q22;
242
Gamma(2,1) =
Q21 - y(5)*Q21 - y(4)*Q11;
243
Gamma(2,2) =
Q22 - y(5)*Q22 - y(4)*Q12;
244
% Vapor pressure from Antoine
245
%For water, the Antoine parameters are
246
%From Aspen
247
% Saturation pressure
248
Psatw = exp(73.649 - 7258./(y(1))-7.3037.*log(y(1)) + 4.1653e-6 .* y(1).^2); % Pa, from Aspen, extended Antoine
249
Psate = exp(73.304 - 7122.3./(y(1)) -7.1424.*log(y(1)) + 2.8853e-6 .*y(1).^2); %Pa, from Aspen
250
%Equilibrium ratio
251
Keq1 =
Psatw*gammaWilson1/P;
252
Keq2 =
Psate*gammaWilson2/P;
253
254
% Transfer rates, 2 component system only kappa (1,1) is necessary. Sum of flux = 0
255
RV(1,1) = y(6)/k_v(1,2) +y(7)/k_v(1,2); % Inverse vapor transfer coefficient
256
RL(1,1) = y(4)/k_l(1,2) + y(5)/k_l(1,2); % inverse liquid transfer coefficient
257
258
kappa_V(1,1) =
RV(1,1)^(-1); % Corrected vapor transfer coefficient
259
kappa_L(1,1) =
RL(1,1)^(-1)*Gamma(1,1); % Corrected liquid transfer coefficient
260
261
J_V1 =
c_TV*kappa_V(1,1)*(x(5)-y(6)); %mol/s m2, water flux in vapor phase
262
J_V2 =
-J_V1; %mol/s m2, ethanol flux in vapor phase
263
J_L1 =
c_TL*kappa_L(1,1)*(y(4)-x(3)); %mol/s m2, water flux in liquid phase
264
J_L2 =
-J_L1; %mol/s m2, ethanol flux in liquid phase
265
266
N_T =
y(2)+y(3); %mol/s, total material transfer rate
267
N_V1 =
aI*J_V1 + x(5) *N_T;
%mol/s, transfer rate of water vapor
268
N_V2 =
aI*J_V2 + x(6) *N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of ethanol vapor
269
N_L1 =
aI*J_L1 + x(3) *N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of water liquid
270
N_L2 =
aI*J_L2 + x(4) *N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of ethanol liquid
271

==PAGE:124==

June, 2018
272
e_L =
aI*h_l*(y(1)-x(1)) + (N_L1*H_Lpar1+N_L2*H_Lpar2); %J/s, heat transfer rate, liquid
273
e_V =
aI*h_v*(x(2)-y(1)) + (N_V1*H_Vpar1+N_V2*H_Vpar2); %J/s, heat transfer rate, vapor
274
%% Diff eqs.
275
dxdt(9,1) = -((1+r_L)*x(15)*H_L - Ljm1*H_Ljm1 - F_L*H_LF + Q_L - e_L); % EjL
276
dxdt(10,1) = -((1+r_V)*x(16)*H_V - Vjp1*H_Vjp1 - F_V*H_VF + Q_V + e_V); % EjV
277
dxdt(11,1) = -((1+r_L)*x(15)*x(3) - Ljm1*xjm11 - f_L1 - N_L1); % M1jL
278
dxdt(12,1) = -((1+r_L)*x(15)*x(4) - Ljm1*xjm12 - f_L2 - N_L2); % M2jL
279
dxdt(13,1) = -((1+r_V)*x(16)*x(5) - Vjp1*yjp11 - f_V1 + N_V1); % M1jV
280
dxdt(14,1) = -((1+r_V)*x(16)*x(6) - Vjp1*yjp12 - f_V2 + N_V2); % M2jV
281
282
dxdt(1,1) = 1/(x(7)*(x(3)*CP_wL+x(4)*CP_eL))*dxdt(9,1); % TjL
283
dxdt(2,1) = 1/(x(8)*(x(5)*CP_wV+x(6)*CP_eV))*dxdt(10,1); % TjV
284
285
dxdt(3,1) = 1/x(7)*dxdt(11,1); % x1j
286
dxdt(4,1) = 1/x(7)*dxdt(12,1);% x2j
287
dxdt(5,1) = 1/x(8)*dxdt(13,1); % y1j
288
dxdt(6,1) = 1/x(8)*dxdt(14,1);% y2j
289
290
dxdt(7,1) = dxdt(11,1)+dxdt(12,1); % MTjL
291
dxdt(8,1) = dxdt(13,1)+dxdt(14,1); % MTjV
292
293
dxdt(15,1) = aC*u_l/Vol * dxdt(7,1); %Lj
294
dxdt(16,1) = aC*u_v/Vol * dxdt(8,1); %Vj
295
sys = dxdt;

and y23

349
r_V = feed(10); %fraction of removed vapor after stage

%From previous stage

%% Properties

% Concentrations, molar flow / volumetric flow

364
c_TV = (x(16))/(aC_V*u_v);
%mol/m3, vapor concentration

rho_wl =
exp(6.9094 - 2.0146*10^(-5)*Tprop - 5.9868*10^(-6)*Tprop^2 + 2.5921*10^(-8)*Tprop^3 - 9.3244*10^(-11)*Tprop^4 + ...
1.2103*10^(-13)*Tprop^5); %kg/m3, density of liquid water
414
rho_wv =

1.5514*10^(-12)*Tprop^5); %kg/m3, density of water vapor

mu_wl =

3.5075*10^(-12)*Tprop^5)*10^(-7); %N s/m, viscosity of water liquid

2.5534*10^(-10)*Tprop^4 - 6.4454*10^(-13)*Tprop^5); %J/m s K, thermal conductivity of liquid water

lambda_wv =

6.9579*10^(-13)*Tprop^5); %J/m s K, thermal conductivity of water vapor

2.2589*10^(-13)*Tprop^5)*10^3; %J/kg K, specific heat capacity of liquid water

Cp_wv =

2.0709*10^(-13)*Tprop^5)*10^3; %J/kg K, specific heat capacity of water vapor

434
Pr_wl =
Cp_wl*mu_wl/lambda_wl; %Prandtl number for liquid phase water
435
Le_wv = Sc_wv/Pr_wv; % Lewis number for vapor phase water
436
Le_wl = Sc_wl/Pr_wl; % Lewis number for liquid phase water
437
438
% From Transport Phenomena: Flat plate geometry
439
%jD_wv =
0.332*Re_wv^(-1/2); %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for diffusion, water vapor
440
%jD_wl =
0.332*Re_wl^(-1/2); %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for diffusion, water liquid
441
%jH_wv =
jD_wv; %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for heat, water vapor
442
%jH_wl =
jD_wl; %Chilton-Colburn j-factor for heat, water liquid
443
444
% Mass and heat transfer coefficient
445
% jD = Sh/(Re Sc^(1/3)), jH = Nu/(Re Pr^(1/3))
446
% Nu = h*l0/lambda, Sh = k*l0/D_AB
447
% flat plane
XVII
==PAGE:126==
A.3. Matlab scripts and s-functions
June, 2018
448
%k_wev =
jD_wv*Re_wv*Sc_wv^(1/3)*D_wav*1/l_0; %m/s, mass transfer coefficient for water in ethanol(air) in vapor phase
449
%k_wel =
jD_wl*Re_wl*Sc_wl^(1/3)*D_wel*1/l_0; %m/s, mass transfer coefficient for water in ethanol liquid phase
450
451
% From Multi component mass transfer. Randomly packed column, Bravo & Fair.
452
k_wev = 50*D_wel*2*Re_wv^(0.7)*Sc_wv^(0.333)*(50*l_0)^(-2); % m/s, ap = 50 m2/m3, dp = 0.005 m, A = 2 for dp<0.012. dp = l_0
453
k_wel = 0.0051*Re_wl^(0.667)*Sc_wl^(-0.5)*(50*l_0)^(0.4)*1/(rho_wl/(mu_wl*9.81))^0.333; %m/s
454
455
% Assigning the mass transfer coefficients in a matrix
456
k_v(1,1) =
0;
457
k_v(1,2) =
k_wev;
458
k_v(2,1) =
k_v(1,2);
459
k_v(2,2) =
0;
460
k_l(1,1) =
0;
461
k_l(1,2) =
k_wel;
462
k_l(2,1) =
k_l(1,2);
463
k_l(2,2) =
0;
464
% Flat plane
465
%h_wv =
jH_wv*Re_wv*Pr_wv^(1/3)*lambda_wv*1/l_0; %J/m2 s K, heat transfer coefficient for water in vapor phase
466
%h_wl =
jH_wl*Re_wl*Pr_wl^(1/3)*lambda_wl*1/l_0; %J/m2 s K, heat transfer coefficient for water in liquid phase
467
468
% Randomly packed column, Chilton-Colburn, sep book
469
h_wv = k_wev*rho_wv*Cp_wv*Le_wv^(2/3); % J/m2 s K
470
h_wl = k_wel*rho_wl*Cp_wl*Le_wl^(1/2); % J/m2 s K
471
472
h_v =
h_wv; % W/m2 K, total heat transfer coefficient in vapor phase
473
h_l =
h_wl; % W/m2 K, total heat transfer coefficient in liquid phase
474
475
% Activity coefficient for binary system
476
% Lambda matrix
477
Lambda(1,1) = 1;
478
Lambda(2,2) = 1;
479
Lambda(1,2) = exp(-0.0503 - 69.6372./y(1)); % From Aspen documentation
480
Lambda(2,1) = exp(-2.5035 + 346.151./y(1)); % From Aspen documentation
481
%S
482
S1 =
y(4)+y(5)*Lambda(1,2);
483
S2 =
y(5)+y(4)*Lambda(2,1);
484
%Q
485
%First composition derivatives
486
Q1 =
-log(S1) - y(4)/S1 - y(5) * Lambda(2,1)/S2;
487
Q2 =
-log(S2) - y(5)/S2 - y(4) * Lambda(1,2)/S1;
488
%Second composition derivatives
489
Q11 =
-2/S1 + y(4)/S1^2 + y(5)*Lambda(2,1)^2/S2^2;
490
Q12 =
-Lambda(1,2)/S1 - Lambda(2,1)/S2 + y(4)*Lambda(1,2)/S1^2 + y(5)*Lambda(2,1)/S2^2;
491
Q21 =
Q12;
492
Q22 =
-2/S2 + y(5)/S2^2 + y(4)*Lambda(1,2)^2/S1^2;
493
%Activity coefficients
494
lngamma1 =
y(4)+y(5)+Q1;
495
lngamma2 =
y(4)+y(5)+Q2;
496
gammaWilson1 =
exp(lngamma1);
497
gammaWilson2 =
exp(lngamma2);
498
% Thermodynamic factor
499
Gamma(1,1) =
Q11 - y(4)*Q11 - y(5)*Q21;
500
Gamma(1,2) =
Q12 - y(4)*Q12 - y(5)*Q22;
501
Gamma(2,1) =
Q21 - y(5)*Q21 - y(4)*Q11;
502
Gamma(2,2) =
Q22 - y(5)*Q22 - y(4)*Q12;
503
504
% Vapor pressure from Antoine
505
%For water, the Antoine parameters are
506
%From Aspen
507
Psatw = exp(73.649 - 7258./(y(1))-7.3037.*log(y(1)) + 4.1653e-6 .* y(1).^2); % Pa, from Aspen, extended Antoine
508
Psate = exp(73.304 - 7122.3./(y(1)) -7.1424.*log(y(1)) + 2.8853e-6 .*y(1).^2); %Pa, from Aspen
509
510
%Equilibrium ratio
511
Keq1 =
Psatw*gammaWilson1/P;
512
Keq2 =
Psate*gammaWilson2/P;
513
514
% Transfer rates, 2 component system only kappa (1,1) is necessary. Sum of flux = 0
515
RV(1,1) = y(6)/k_v(1,2) +y(7)/k_v(1,2); % Inverse vapor transfer coefficient
516
RL(1,1) = y(4)/k_l(1,2) + y(5)/k_l(1,2); % inverse liquid transfer coefficient
517
518
kappa_V(1,1) =
RV(1,1)^(-1); % Corrected vapor transfer coefficient
519
kappa_L(1,1) =
RL(1,1)^(-1)*Gamma(1,1); % Corrected liquid transfer coefficient
520
521
J_V1 =
c_TV*kappa_V(1,1)*(x(5)-y(6)); %mol/s m2, water flux in vapor phase
522
J_V2 =
-J_V1; %mol/s m2, ethanol flux in vapor phase
523
J_L1 =
c_TL*kappa_L(1,1)*(y(4)-x(3)); %mol/s m2, water flux in liquid phase
524
J_L2 =
-J_L1; %mol/s m2, ethanol flux in liquid phase
525
526
N_T =
y(2)+y(3); %mol/s, total material transfer rate
527
N_V1 =
aI*J_V1 + x(5) *N_T;
%mol/s, transfer rate of water vapor
528
N_V2 =
aI*J_V2 + x(6) *N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of ethanol vapor
529
N_L1 =
aI*J_L1 + x(3) *N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of water liquid
530
N_L2 =
aI*J_L2 + x(4) *N_T; %mol/s, transfer rate of ethanol liquid
531
532
e_L =
aI*h_l*(y(1)-x(1)) + (N_L1*H_Lpar1+N_L2*H_Lpar2); %J/s, heat transfer rate, liquid
533
e_V =
aI*h_v*(x(2)-y(1)) + (N_V1*H_Vpar1+N_V2*H_Vpar2); %J/s, heat transfer rate, vapor
534
%% Algebraic eqs
535
f(1,1) = e_V - e_L; %EI
536
f(2,1) = y(2) - N_L1; %RL1
537
f(3,1) = y(2) - N_V1; %RV1
538
f(4,1) = y(4) + y(5) - 1; %SxI
539
f(5,1) = y(6) + y(7) -1; %SyI
XVIII
==PAGE:127==
A.3. Matlab scripts and s-functions
June, 2018
540
f(6,1) = Keq1 * y(4) - y(6); %Q1I
541
f(7,1) = Keq2 * y(5) - y(7); %Q2I
542
sys = [x;f];

function sys=mdlOutputs(t,x,u)

%% Sorting inputs
42

44
feed = u(12:18); % feed, duty, removed flow rates
45
preL= u(19:22); % liquid from previous stage
46

47
48
R = par(1); % J/K mol, gas constant
49
P = par(2); % Pa, stage pressure
50
Vol = par(3); % m3, stage volume
51
tres = par(4); % s, residence time
52
Fj = feed(1); % mol/s, total feed flow
53
z1 = feed(2); % water fraction of feed
54
z2 = feed(3); % ethanol fraction of feed
55

56
U = feed(5); % liquid removed
57
W = feed(6); % vapor removed
58
Q = feed(7); % J/s, heat duty, (-) if energy added, (+) if energy removed
59
Ljm1 = preL(1); % mol/s, liquid from stage j-1
60
Tjm1 = preL(2); % K, temperature of previous liquid stage
61
xjm11 = preL(3); % water fraction in previous liquid stage
62
xjm12 = preL(4); % ethanol fraction in previous liquid stage
63
Vjp1 = preV(1); % mol/s, vapor flow rate from previous stage
64
Tjp1 = preV(2); % K, temperature of vapor from previous stage
65
yjp11 = preV(3); % water fraction in vapor from previous stage
66
yjp12 = preV(4); % ethanol fraction in vapor from previous stage
67
%% Properties
68
% Enthalpies
69
% Feed
70
H_wLF = 40660 + 75*(TF-373.15); %J/mol, enthalpy of water liquid feed
71
H_eLF = 38600 + 112.4*(TF-352); %J/mol, enthalpy of ethanol liquid feed
72
H_wVF = 40660 + 27*(TF-373.15); %J/mol enthalpy of water vapor feed
73
H_eVF = 38600 + 87.53*(TF-352); %J/mol, enthalpy of ethanol vapor feed
74
if Fj == 0 % If statement to avoid division by zero.
75
H_LF = 0;
76
else
XIX
==PAGE:128==
A.3. Matlab scripts and s-functions
June, 2018
77
H_LF = H_wLF*z1+ H_eLF*z2; %J/mol, total liquid feed enthalpy
78
end
79
if Fj == 0 % If statement to avoid division by 0.
80
H_VF = 0;
81
else
82
H_VF = H_wVF*z1 + H_eVF*z2; %J/mol, total vapor feed enthalpy
83
end
84
HF = H_LF + H_VF; % J/mol
85
86
% Previous stages
87
H_Ljm11 =
40660 + 75*(Tjm1-373.15); %J/mol, enthalpy of water liquid from previous stage
88
H_Ljm12 = 38600 + 112.4*(Tjm1-352); %J/mol, enthalpy of ethanol liquid from prevoius stage
89
H_Vjp11 = 40660 + 27*(Tjp1-373.15); %J/mol enthalpy of water vapor from prevoius stage
90
H_Vjp12 = 38600 + 87.53*(Tjp1-352); %J/mol, enthalpy of ethanol vapor from prevoius stage
91

92
Hjp1 = H_Vjp11*yjp11 + H_Vjp12*yjp12; %J/mol enthalpy of vapor from previous stage
93
94
% Current stage
95
H_L = y(4) * (40660 + 75*(y(3)-373.15)) + y(5) * (38600 + 112.4*(y(3)-352)); %J/mol, liquid enthalpy
96
H_V = y(6) * (40660 + 27*(y(3)-373.15)) + y(7) * (38600 + 87.53*(y(3)-352)); %J/mol, vapor enthalpy
97
98
% Equilibrium ratio
99
% Wilson
100
101
% Lambda matrix
102
Lambda(1,1) = 1;
103
Lambda(2,2) = 1;
104
Lambda(1,2) = exp(-0.0503 - 69.6372./y(3)); % From Aspen documentation
105
Lambda(2,1) = exp(-2.5035 + 346.151./y(3)); % From Aspen documentation
106
107
% S
108
S1 =
y(4)+y(5)*Lambda(1,2);
109
S2 =
y(5)+y(4)*Lambda(2,1);
110
111
% Q
112
% First composition derivatives
113
Q1 =
-log(S1) - y(4)/S1 - y(5) * Lambda(2,1)/S2;
114
Q2 =
-log(S2) - y(5)/S2 - y(4) * Lambda(1,2)/S1;
115
% Second composition derivatives
116
Q11 =
-2/S1 + y(4)/S1^2 + y(5)*Lambda(2,1)^2/S2^2;
117
Q12 =
-Lambda(1,2)/S1 - Lambda(2,1)/S2 + y(4)*Lambda(1,2)/S1^2 + y(5)*Lambda(2,1)/S2^2;
118
Q21 =
Q12;
119
Q22 =
-2/S2 + y(5)/S2^2 + y(4)*Lambda(1,2)^2/S1^2;
120
% Activity coefficients
121
lngamma1 =
y(4)+y(5)+Q1;
122
lngamma2 =
y(4)+y(5)+Q2;
123
gammaWilson1 =
exp(lngamma1);
124
gammaWilson2 =
exp(lngamma2);
125
126
% Vapor pressure
127
Psatw = exp(73.649 - 7258./(y(3))-7.3037.*log(y(3)) + 4.1653e-6 .* y(3).^2); % Pa, from Aspen, extended Antoine
128
Psate = exp(73.304 - 7122.3./(y(3)) -7.1424.*log(y(3)) + 2.8853e-6 .*y(3).^2); %Pa, from Aspen
129
130
% K-values
131
Keq1 = Psatw*gammaWilson1/P; % K-value, water
132
Keq2 = Psate*gammaWilson2/P; % K-value, ethanol
133
%% Equations
134
% y =[Lj Vj Tj x1 x2 y1 y2];
135
F(1) = Ljm1*xjm11 + Vjp1*yjp11 + Fj*z1 - (y(1)+U)*y(4) - (y(2)+W)*y(6); %M1j
136
F(2) = Ljm1*xjm12 + Vjp1*yjp12 + Fj*z2 - (y(1)+U)*y(5) - (y(2)+W)*y(7); %M2j
137
F(3) = y(6) - Keq1*y(4); %E1j
138
F(4) = y(7) - Keq2*y(5); %E2j
139
F(5) = y(6) + y(7) - 1; %Sy
140
F(6) = y(4) + y(5) - 1; %Sx
141
F(7) = Ljm1*Hjm1 + Vjp1*Hjp1 + Fj*HF - (y(1)+U)*H_L - (y(2)+W)*H_V - Q; %Hj
142
143
sys = F;

Keq1 = Psatw*gammaWilson1/P; % K-value, water
134
Keq2 = Psate*gammaWilson2/P; % K-value, ethanol
135

136
% x = [Lj Vj Tj x1 x2 y1 y2 Hj
M1j M2j];
137

x9
x10];
138
F(1) = Ljm1*xjm11 + Vjp1*yjp11 + Fj*z1 - (y(1)+U)*y(4) - (y(2)+W)*y(6); %M1j
139
F(2) = Ljm1*xjm12 + Vjp1*yjp12 + Fj*z2 - (y(1)+U)*y(5) - (y(2)+W)*y(7); %M2j
140
F(3) = y(6) - Keq1*y(4); %E1j
141
F(4) = y(7) - Keq2*y(5); %E2j
142
F(5) = y(6) + y(7) - 1; %Sy
143
F(6) = y(4) + y(5) - 1; %Sx
144
F(7) = Ljm1*Hjm1 + Vjp1*Hjp1 + Fj*HF - (y(1)+U)*H_L - (y(2)+W)*H_V - Q; %Hj
145
F(8) = y(8) - (H_L*c_TL + H_V*c_TV)*Vol; % Energy holdup (J)
146
F(9) = y(9) - (y(4)*c_TL + y(6)*c_TV)*Vol; % Water holdup (mol)
147
F(10) = y(10) - (y(5)*c_TL + y(7)*c_TV)*Vol; %Ethanol holdup (mol)
148
sys = F;
